Unseen Matters

The Infinite Regress of Gratitude and the Necessity of Revelation
Nasafi proves the impossibility of fulfilling gratitude without revelation due to infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why human reason alone cannot determine the proper form of gratitude, and why revelation is necessary to specify the required act of gratitude.

Core Idea
Gratitude requires revelation; reason alone leads to an infinite regress.

Nasafi presents a powerful argument that without divine guidance, the obligation of gratitude is impossible to fulfill. He shows that any attempt to thank God creates a new blessing requiring further thanks, leading to an endless chain. Additionally, human intellect is incapable of knowing the manner, quantity, and subtlety of gratitude. Only revelation can provide the necessary specification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the chain of reasoning: impossibility → infinite regress → need for specification → intellectual incapacity → necessity of revelation.
2
Identify the infinite regress argument
The passage argues that gratitude itself requires divine enablement (tawfīq), which is a new blessing requiring further gratitude. Trace this chain and see why it never ends.
This shows why human effort alone can never fulfill the obligation.
3
Understand the role of divine enablement (tawfīq)
Tawfīq is God's blessing that enables gratitude. But it is itself a blessing, so it demands its own thanks. This creates an endless cycle.
4
See why intellect is insufficient
The passage lists four things intellect cannot grasp: the manner (kayfiyya), quantity (kamiyya), goodness (ḥusn), and subtlety (diqqa) of gratitude. Understand each.
This reinforces the need for revelation.
5
Grasp the necessity of revealed explanation (bayān samʿī)
The conclusion is that only a revealed statement can specify what gratitude is required. The 'wise master' analogy confirms this: a command without explanation is unjust and unwise.
This argument is foundational for the necessity of prophets.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا كان الأمر على ما قررنا لن يتصور من العبد أن يأتي بشكر يكافئ أدنى نعمة من النعم فضلاً عن أن يكون مكافئًا لما تقلب هو فيه من نعمه في جميع عمره. يحققه أنه لا يتمكن من أداء شكر نعمه إلا بتوفيق من الله وتيسير منه على ذلك، وذلك نعمة جديدة يجب بمقابلتها الشكر، ولا يتوصل إلى شكرها إلا بأثر آخر للتوفيق، وهكذا إلى ما لا يتناهى. فإذا على هذه القضية لا يمكن للعبد الخروج عن شكر نعمة واحدة وإن استغرق جميع عمره لشكرها، فكيف يمكنه إذا شكر جميع نعمه؟! والشكر واجب عليه فلابد من بيان قدر الشكر الذي يستوجبه الله تعالى على عباده على نعمه ليتمكنوا من أداء ذلك. وكذا العقول قاصرة عن كيفية شكر كل نعمة كما هي عاجزة عن الوقوف على كميته وكذا هي قاصرة عن الوقوف على حسن الشكر ودقته فلابد من بيان سمعي يرد بذلك كله ليمكن للعباد التقصي عما لزمهم من عهدة تكليف أداء الشكر أن الحكيم إذا أمر عبدًا من عبيده بشيء لابد من يبين ذلك بيانا يتمكن العبد من الائتمار إذ لولا البيان لكان ذلك تكليف ما ليس في وسع المكلف الائتمار له وهو خارج عن الحكمة.
And if the matter is as we have established, it is inconceivable for the servant to bring gratitude equal to the least of the blessings, let alone to be equal to all the blessings in which he is immersed throughout his entire life. This is confirmed by the fact that he is not able to perform gratitude for His blessings except through divine enablement (tawfīq) from God and facilitation from Him for that, and that is a new blessing for which gratitude is obligatory in return, and he cannot attain gratitude for it except through another effect of divine enablement, and so on ad infinitum. So on this basis, it is not possible for the servant to exit from [the obligation of] gratitude for a single blessing even if he spends his entire life thanking it, so how can he thank all his blessings? And gratitude is obligatory upon him, so there must be a specification of the measure of gratitude that God Almighty requires from His servants for His blessings so that they can perform that. Likewise, intellects are incapable concerning the manner (kayfiyya) of thanking every blessing, just as they are incapable of standing on its quantity (kamiyya), and likewise they are incapable of standing on the goodness (ḥusn) and subtlety (diqqa) of gratitude. Therefore, there must be a revealed explanation (bayān sam'ī) that conveys all of that so that the servants can discharge the obligation of performing gratitude incumbent upon them. For if a wise master commands a servant of his to do something, he must explain it in a way that enables the servant to obey; otherwise that would be an obligation of what is not within the capacity of the obligated to obey, and that is outside wisdom.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the necessity of divine revelation (sam'ī bayān) to define the required gratitude. It begins by restating the conclusion from the previous argument: it is impossible for a servant to perform gratitude equal even to the least blessing, let alone all blessings. The proof lies in the fact that gratitude itself requires divine enablement (tawfīq), which itself is a new blessing requiring further gratitude, leading to an infinite regress. Thus, one cannot escape the obligation of a single blessing even by spending a lifetime thanking it. Since gratitude is obligatory, God must specify the measure (qadr) of gratitude He requires. Additionally, human intellects are incapable of determining the manner (kayfiyya), quantity (kamiyya), and even the moral goodness (ḥusn) and subtlety (diqqa) of gratitude. Therefore, a revealed statement (bayān sam'ī) is necessary. This is further supported by a general principle: a wise master must explain a command in a way that the servant can obey; otherwise it would be an impossible obligation (taklīf mā lā yutāq), which is contrary to wisdom (ḥikma). Thus, the argument establishes the indispensable role of revelation in determining religious duties.

Key Terms
توفيق
بيان سمعي
تكليف
حكمة
كيفية
كمية
حسن
دقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think reason alone can determine the proper form of gratitude.
Correction: This passage shows that without revelation, it is impossible due to infinite regress and intellectual incapacity.

Checkpoint
What does the infinite regress argument demonstrate?
A) That gratitude is impossible
B) That divine enablement (tawfiq) is required for every act of gratitude, making human effort unnecessary
C) That without revelation, the required measure and manner of gratitude cannot be known
D) That reason is sufficient for moral obligation

Key Takeaway
Without revelation, the obligation of gratitude is impossible to fulfill; revelation specifies the required act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the infinite regress argument, do you truly believe that your own efforts at gratitude are insufficient unless guided by revelation? How does that realization affect the way you approach your daily acts of worship and thanks to God? Will you start actively seeking the revealed standards for gratitude rather than relying on your own feelings?
?
The passage says that even one lifetime of gratitude is not enough for a single blessing. Honestly, how often do you pause to thank God for even the most obvious blessings like health or faith? If you knew that every moment of thankfulness is itself a new blessing requiring more gratitude, would that change your urgency in seeking God's guidance through revelation?
?
This lesson argues that reason alone cannot determine the correct way to express gratitude. Have you ever caught yourself justifying a way of worship based solely on what 'makes sense' to you rather than on what God has specifically commanded? What will you do today to submit your intellect to the revealed guidance in matters of gratitude and worship?

The Five Subdivisions of Transactions in Islamic Law
Enumeration of the five types of transactions with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five subdivisions of transactions in Islamic law as enumerated by Imam al-Nasafi, along with examples of each.

Core Idea
Transactions in Islamic law split into five clear branches.

Imam al-Nasafi provides a concise classification of transactions (mu'āmalāt) into five categories. This categorization helps organize the vast field of Islamic commercial and civil law. In this passage, each subdivision is defined with concrete examples from everyday life.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage silently, then read the English translation. Notice how the author introduces the five categories with the phrase 'تنقسم إلى خمسة'.
The passage is a straightforward enumeration without argumentation.
2
Identify each subdivision
As you read, list the five subdivisions in your mind: financial negotiations, marriage relations, litigations, trusts, and inheritances. Say them out loud to reinforce memory.
Each subdivision comes with one or two examples—note these well.
3
Connect to real life
Think of a real situation that fits into each category. For example, renting an apartment falls under financial negotiations; a court case over a property dispute is a litigation. This will help you see the practical scope of mu'āmalāt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[أقسام المعاملات]: والمعاملات تنقسم إلى خمسة: وهي المفاوضات المالية كالبيوع والإجارات، والمناكحات كالتزويج والتطليق، والمخاصمات كالدعاوي والبينات، والأمانات كالودائع والعواري، والتركات كالوصايا والمواريث.
[The divisions of transactions]: Transactions are divided into five: financial negotiations like sales and leases, marriage relations like marriage and divorce, litigations like claims and evidences, trusts like deposits and loans, and inheritances like bequests and inheritances.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage enumerates the five subdivisions of transactions (mu'āmalāt). Each subdivision is defined with examples: financial dealings (buying, leasing), marriage relations (marriage, divorce), lawsuits (claims, evidence), trusts (deposits, loans), and inheritances (bequests, inheritance). This is a straightforward enumeration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the five subdivisions of transactions according to this passage?
A) Financial negotiations
B) Marriage relations
C) Penalties
D) Trusts

Key Takeaway
Islamic law divides transactions into five categories: financial dealings, marriages, litigations, trusts, and inheritances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You interact with transactions daily—buying, renting, maybe even marriage. Did you previously see all these as part of a divine legal framework? How will this categorization change the intentionality behind your next financial or family decision?
?
This lesson shows that Islamic law does not separate the 'sacred' from the 'mundane'—even sales and divorces are categorized as acts of worship (mu'āmalāt). Is there an area of your life you have been treating as purely worldly that you now need to realign with this perspective?
?
If you were to classify your own daily dealings into these five categories, which category do you neglect most in terms of seeking Islamic knowledge? Commit to studying one ruling from that category this week.

Five Subdivisions of Penalties
Enumeration of the five types of penalties in Islamic law.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five categories of penalties (zajājir) and their corresponding punishments as outlined by Imam al-Nasafi.

Core Idea
Penalties in Islamic law are classified into five categories based on the nature of the crime.

Imam al-Nasafi provides a concise enumeration of the five types of penalties (zajājir) in Islamic jurisprudence. Each penalty corresponds to a specific crime, ranging from murder to apostasy. This classification helps in understanding the systematic nature of Islamic criminal law. Let's examine each category.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation to get an overview of the five penalties.
Notice the structure: each penalty is introduced by 'مزجرة'.
2
Identify each crime-penalty pair
For each of the five categories, note the specific crime (e.g., killing the self) and the corresponding punishment (e.g., qisas or diyah).
3
Learn key Arabic terms
Familiarize yourself with the Arabic terms for each penalty, such as القود, الدية, القطع, الصلب, الجلد, الرجم, التفسيق, رد الشهادات, and الردة.
These terms are essential for understanding Islamic legal texts.
4
Reflect on the classification principle
Consider why penalties are grouped this way: by the type of harm (killing, taking property, violating modesty, defaming honor, abandoning religion).
This shows the comprehensive nature of Islamic law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[أقسام المزاجر]: والمزاجر تنقسم أيضا إلى خمسة أقسام: وهي مزجرة قتل النفس كالقود والدية، ومزجرة أخذ المال كالقطع أو الصلب، ومزجرة هتك الستر كالجلد أو الرجم، ومزجرة ثلب العرض كالجلد مع التفسيق ورد الشهادات على التأبيد، ومزجرة خلع البيضة كالقتل على الردة.
[The divisions of penalties]: Penalties are also divided into five sections: The penalty for killing the self like retaliation (qiṣāṣ) and blood-money (diya), the penalty for taking property like amputation or crucifixion, the penalty for violating modesty like flogging or stoning, the penalty for defaming honor like flogging with disparagement and rejection of testimony forever, and the penalty for abandoning the religion like execution for apostasy.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage enumerates the five categories of penalties (zajājir). Each penalty is specified with its corresponding punishment: murder (retaliation or blood-money), theft (amputation or crucifixion), adultery (flogging or stoning), false accusation of adultery (flogging plus lifelong testimony rejection), and apostasy (execution). This is a clear enumeration.

Key Terms
المزاجر
القود
الدية
القطع
الصلب
الجلد
الرجم
التفسيق
رد الشهادات
الردة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Penalties in Islamic law are exclusively physical punishments like flogging or amputation.
Correction: The passage shows that penalties also include social sanctions like lifelong rejection of testimony, and financial penalties like blood money.

Checkpoint
Which penalty does the author list for the crime of defaming honor (ثلب العرض)?
A) Flogging alone
B) Flogging with disparagement and rejection of testimony forever
C) Amputation
D) Execution

Key Takeaway
Islamic penalties are systematically classified into five categories, each addressing a specific type of crime with proportionate punishments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were guilty of defaming someone's honor (qadhf), the penalty includes not only flogging but also being labeled corrupt and losing your testimony forever. How would that social and spiritual consequence affect your relationship with the community and with Allah? What steps would you take to avoid ever committing such a sin?
?
The penalty for apostasy is execution. Why does Islam place such a severe penalty on leaving the faith? How does this reality strengthen your own commitment to Islam, and what can you do today to protect your iman against doubts?
?
Think about the five essential goods that these penalties protect: life, property, modesty, honor, and religion. Which of these do you most take for granted? What specific action will you take this week to safeguard that good in your own life or in the life of another Muslim?

The Four Categories of Etiquettes and Traits
Classification of etiquettes and traits into four subdivisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four subdivisions of etiquettes and traits as defined by Imam al-Nasafi.

Core Idea
Etiquettes and traits are divided into four categories: morals, manners, policies, and sociability.

Imam al-Nasafi begins his discussion of the fifth category of sam‘iyyat by breaking it down into four distinct parts. This passage presents a simple but foundational classification that organizes human conduct and character into manageable categories. Understanding this division helps the student grasp the breadth of Islamic teachings on behavior.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic sentence and its English translation carefully. Notice the four terms listed.
The passage is short but packs a complete classification.
2
Identify the four categories
List the four subdivisions: morals (akhlāq), manners (ādāb), policies (siyāsāt), and sociability (mu‘āsharāt).
These terms have specific meanings in Islamic ethics.
3
Understand the classification
Reflect on why these four categories cover different aspects of behavior: inner character, outward conduct, governance, and social interaction.
This classification helps in organizing Islamic teachings on ethics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والآداب والشيم تنقسم أربعة أقسام: أحدها: الأخلاق والثاني: الآداب، والثالث: السياسات والرابع: المعاشرات.
And etiquettes and traits are divided into four sections: one of them: morals, the second: manners, the third: policies, and the fourth: sociability.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage classifies the fifth category (etiquettes and traits) into four subdivisions: morals (akhlāq), manners (ādāb), policies (siyāsāt), and sociability (mu‘āsharāt). This is a simple enumeration.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Three
B) Four
C) Five
D) Two

Key Takeaway
Etiquettes and traits are classified into four interrelated types: morals, manners, policies, and sociability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of the four categories—morals, manners, policies, or sociability—do you find most lacking in your daily life, and what one concrete action will you take today to strengthen it?
?
Think of a recent interaction where your inner morals and outward manners were not aligned. How can you use the lesson's categories to bring them into harmony?

Faith in the Revealed Books
Author affirms faith in revealed books and lists four categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that faith in Allah's books includes believing in all scriptures revealed to the prophets, specifically the scriptures (ṣuḥuf), Torah, Gospel, and Psalms, as mentioned in the Qur'an.

Core Idea
Belief in all revealed books, not just the Qur'an, is obligatory.

This lesson focuses on the third pillar of iman: faith in the revealed books (al-īmān bi-l-kutub). The author, Imam al-Nasafi, briefly affirms this obligation by referring to two previously cited verses and explicitly naming the scriptures, Torah, Gospel, and Psalms. Though concise, this passage establishes that belief in all divine scriptures is a necessary part of a Muslim's faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic sentence and its translation. Notice that it is a short but complete statement that affirms faith in the books.
The passage is concise; every word carries meaning.
2
Identify the key books mentioned
The passage lists four categories: scriptures (ṣuḥuf), Torah, Gospel, and Psalms. Note that the Qur'an is not explicitly mentioned here because it is the overarching revelation.
The term 'ṣuḥuf' often refers to the scrolls of Abraham and Moses.
3
Understand the source of obligation
The author refers to 'the two verses' mentioned earlier. These are likely from Surah al-Baqarah (2:285) and Surah al-Nisa (4:136), which command belief in Allah, His angels, books, and messengers.
This shows that the obligation is rooted in the Qur'an.
4
Reflect on the implication
Why is it important to name these books specifically? It emphasizes that believers must affirm all previous scriptures, not just the Qur'an, as they are from Allah.
5
Check your understanding
Take the checkpoint quiz to test your recall of the books mentioned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الإيمان بالكتب فقد ورد به له على ما ذكرنا من الآيتين، وقد أفصح بذكر الصحف وذكر التوراة والإنجيل والزبور على ما لا يخفى.
As for faith in the books, it has been reported to him according to what we mentioned from the two verses, and He has made clear by mentioning the scriptures (ṣuḥuf) and mentioning the Torah (al-Tawrāh), the Gospel (al-Injīl), and the Psalms (al-Zabūr), as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This is a concise definition and proof for the third pillar of iman: faith in God's books (al-īmān bi-l-kutub). The author refers to the two previously cited verses (from Surah al-Baqarah 285 and Surah al-Nisa 136) as the source of obligation. He then notes that the Qur'an explicitly mentions the scriptures (ṣuḥuf, likely of Abraham and Moses), the Torah, the Gospel, and the Psalms, indicating that faith in these books is required. The phrase 'as is not hidden' suggests this is well-known from the Qur'an. No further detail is given; the passage serves as a brief affirmation within a larger enumeration of the articles of faith.

Key Terms
الإيمان بالكتب
الصحف
التوراة
الإنجيل
الزبور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that only the Qur'an is required to be believed in as a divine book.
Correction: This passage shows that belief in previous scriptures (ṣuḥuf, Torah, Gospel, Psalms) is also obligatory, as they are explicitly mentioned in the Qur'an.

Checkpoint
Which books are explicitly mentioned in this passage as part of faith in the revealed books?
A) The Qur'an and the Torah
B) The scriptures (suhuf), Torah, Gospel, and Psalms
C) Only the Torah and Gospel
D) The Psalms and the Qur'an

Key Takeaway
Faith in Allah's books requires believing in all scriptures He revealed, including the scriptures, Torah, Gospel, and Psalms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Qur'an, do you ever reflect that it confirms the same truth that was sent to Moses, Jesus, and David? How does that change your relationship with those earlier prophets?
?
If you were to meet a Jew or Christian who follows their scripture sincerely, would you still consider them misguided? What does your belief in the original Torah and Gospel require of you in that encounter?
?
The author says belief in the books is 'not hidden' in the Qur'an. But is it truly alive in your heart? Do you feel the weight of responsibility to honour all of Allah's revelations, or do you treat the earlier ones as irrelevant?

Two Objections to Bodily Resurrection and Their Answers
Nasafi presents two objections against bodily resurrection and answers them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main objections raised against bodily resurrection and the rational responses given by Nasafi.

Core Idea
Resurrection restores original parts, not food; its purpose is known only to God.

The deniers of bodily resurrection presented two powerful objections: the cannibalism paradox and the purposelessness argument. Nasafi responds by clarifying the nature of the restored parts and rejecting the assumption that every act of God must have a knowable purpose. This lesson presents these objections and answers as a case study in using both transmitted and rational proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then the translation. Let the author's own arguments come through before you analyse.
Notice the repeated line in the Arabic; the lesson uses the corrected version.
2
Identify the first objection
Find where Nasafi presents the cannibalism scenario and understand the problem: if one person eats another, the particles cannot be restored in both.
3
Identify the second objection
Look for the argument about purpose: resurrection either has no purpose (frivolous) or a purpose that is either for God (impossible) or for the servant (pain or pleasure, both rejected).
4
Examine the answers
For the first objection, note the distinction between original parts and food particles. For the second, note the rejection of a knowable purpose and the questioning of pleasure as mere removal of pain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الإيمان بالبعث فقد ورد به عليه السلام ونطق به ما ورد به من كتابه وأثبت ذلك بالحجج العقلية على ما مر ذكره قبل هذا، وهو أمر به ينفصل عن أصحاب الديانة من المجان والخلعاء، وأن كل من آمن بنبي من الأنبياء فقد صدق بالبعث وفي الجحد به إيمان النفس الأمارة بالسوء عن عواقب المطالبات، وتسويغ لها عامة ما تشتهيه من الفواحش وتنجذب إليه همته من المأثم والقبائح. وأما الكلام في صورة الحشر والثواب ففيه بين الناس اختلاف كبير، وفي الأقاويل وشبهها وكيفية وجوه ردها وتحقيق ما أتى به الرسول صلوات جميع الله عليه طول فأعرضنا عن ذكرها. بيان: وأما الكلام في صورة الحشر والثواب ففيه بين الناس اختلاف كبير، وفي الأقاويل وشبهها وكيفية وجوه ردها وتحقيق ما أتى به الرسول صلوات جميع الله عليه طول فأعرضنا عن ذكرها. بيان: وأما الكلام في صورة الحشر والثواب ففيه بين الناس اختلاف كبير، وفي الأقاويل وشبهها وكيفية وجوه ردها وتحقيق ما أتى به الرسول صلوات جميع الله عليه طول فأعرضنا عن ذكرها. بيان والمنكرون يحتجون بأمرين: الأول: لو أكل إنسان إنسانا بحيث صار المأكول جزءا منه فتلك الأجزاء إما أن تعاد فيهما، وهو محال، أو في أحدهما فلا يكون الآخر معادا بعينه، ويجاب عن هذا بأن المعاد هو الأجزاء الأصلية وهي الباقية من أول العمر إلى آخره، وهذه في الأكل فضل فإنا نعلم أن الإنسان باق مدة عمره وأجزاء الغذاء تتوارد عليه وتزول عنه. الثاني: لو حشر فإما لا لغرض وهو عبث، وإما لغرض، وهو إما عائد إلى الله وهو منزه عنه، أو إلى العبد، وهو إما الإيلام وإنه منتف إجماعا وببديهة العقل لقبحه وعدم ملاءمته والعناية، وإما الإلذاذ وهو أيضا باطل لأن اللذة إنما هي دفع الألم بالاستقراء وإنه للحكمة لو ترك لم يكن له ألم والإيلام ليدفع فيلتذ لا يصلح غرضا إذ لا معنى له. ويجاب عن هذا أنا نختار أنه لا لغرض ولا عبث ولا قبح عقلي على الله تعالى - كذلك لا نسلم أن الغرض هو إما الإيلام أو الإلذاذ ولعل فيه غرضا آخر لا نعلمه. ثم سلمنا لكن لا نسلم أن اللذة دفع الألم، غايته أن في دفع الألم لذة.. ثم سلمنا أن ذلك في اللذات الدنيوية فلم قلتم إن اللذات الأخروية كذلك، ولم لا يجوز أن
As for belief in the resurrection, it has been transmitted by the Prophet (peace be upon him) and spoken by what was transmitted from His Book, and he established it by rational proofs as mentioned before this. It is a command by which he separates from the people of religion among the insane and the wicked. And whoever believes in a prophet among the prophets has affirmed the resurrection, and in denying it there is giving free rein to the soul that commands evil from the consequences of demands, and permitting it all the obscenities it desires and the sins and abominations to which its ambition is attracted. As for speech about the form of the gathering and reward, there is great disagreement among people concerning it, and regarding the sayings and their likenesses, and the manner of refuting them and verifying what the Messenger (peace be upon all of God's blessings upon him) brought, we have refrained from mentioning it. Explanation: The deniers argue with two matters: First: If one person eats another such that the eaten becomes part of him, then those parts are either restored in both—which is impossible—or in one of them, so the other is not restored in his very self. The answer to this is that what is restored are the original parts, which remain from the beginning of life to its end, while what is eaten is surplus, for we know that a person remains throughout his lifespan while food parts come and go. Second: If [the dead] are gathered, then either it is without purpose, which is frivolous, or it is with a purpose. The purpose either goes back to God—and He is exalted above that—or to the servant, and it is either infliction of pain, which is universally rejected and by intuitive reason due to its ugliness and lack of suitability and care, or it is pleasure, which is also invalid because pleasure is only the removal of pain by induction, and it is for wisdom; if it were left, he would not have pain, and inflicting pain to remove it so that he experiences pleasure is not a valid purpose, for it has no meaning. The answer to this is that we choose that it is without purpose nor frivolous, and there is no rational ugliness on God—likewise we do not concede that the purpose is either infliction of pain or pleasure; perhaps there is another purpose we do not know. Then we concede, but we do not concede that pleasure is the removal of pain; at most, there is pleasure in the removal of pain. Then we concede that this is the case for worldly pleasures, but why do you say that otherworldly pleasures are likewise, and why is it not permissible that
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two objections against bodily resurrection. The author first affirms that belief in resurrection is established by revelation and rational proofs, and he notes its moral importance as a deterrent from sin. He then explains that he will not detail the various opinions on the manner of gathering. Instead, he presents two objections raised by deniers and provides answers. The first objection concerns a case of cannibalism: if one person eats another, the same particles cannot be restored in both bodies simultaneously. The answer distinguishes between original parts and food particles; resurrection restores the original parts that persist throughout life, not the transient food. The second objection questions the purpose of resurrection: if without purpose it is frivolous, and if with purpose it must be either for God (impossible) or for the servant (either for pain or pleasure, both rejected). The answer rejects the need for a purpose for God, denies that the only possible purposes are pain or pleasure (there could be another unknown purpose), and also questions the assumption that pleasure is merely removal of pain, especially for otherworldly pleasures. The passage ends while the author is continuing his argument.

Key Terms
حشر الأجساد
الأجزاء الأصلية
غرض
عبث
قبح عقلي
اللذة
دفع الألم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume that the objection from cannibalism is decisive because they think every particle must be restored.
Correction: Nasafi clarifies that only original parts (الأجزاء الأصلية) are restored, not transient food particles, so the objection fails.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what objects are restored in the resurrection?
A) All particles of the body.
B) The original parts that persist from the beginning of life to its end.
C) Both the original parts and food particles.
D) Only the soul.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi defends bodily resurrection by distinguishing between original and transient parts, and by rejecting anthropomorphic purpose for divine acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of your own body being resurrected, do you focus on the physical particles or the identity that God preserves? What does that reveal about where you place your trust for the afterlife?
?
Have you ever expected God to justify His commands to you, as if He needs a purpose you can understand? How does this lesson challenge you to submit to divine wisdom even when it is hidden?
?
If bodily resurrection is real and you will stand before God, what pleasure or pain are you accumulating today that will be restored in a form beyond your current imagination?

The Fourth Wisdom: Prayer Removes Sins
The author presents the fourth wisdom of prayer as a means of repentance and forgiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how prayer functions as a comprehensive act of repentance and purification from sins according to Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Prayer is the ultimate act of repentance that removes sins.

This lesson explores the fourth wisdom of prayer: its role in removing sins. The author presents prayer as the comprehensive means by which a servant seeks forgiveness and purification. We'll examine the passage that explains how prayer embodies repentance, supplication, and obedience, supported by a Quranic verse and the universal practice of prayer among prophets.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Pay attention to the flow: the author states the problem (inevitable sins), then the solution (prayer as comprehensive repentance), and concludes with a Quranic proof and historical observation.
Notice the phrase 'النهاية في استجماع ذلك' – prayer gathers all aspects of repentance.
2
Identify elements of repentance in prayer
The passage lists several elements that prayer comprises: returning to Allah's remembrance, returning to His service, supplication, showing piety, and obedience. List them and reflect on how each is part of prayer.
These elements show that prayer is not merely ritual but a holistic act of worship.
3
Connect the Quranic verse and the universal practice
Read the verse from Surah Hud (11:114) and consider how it supports the argument. Also reflect on the author's claim that no prophetic community was without prayer – this underscores its essential role in repentance.
The verse directly states that good deeds (like prayer) remove bad deeds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرابع: أن العبد في أوقات ليله ونهاره لا يسلم من صغائر الذلل وأنواع الخطايا، وحكم العبودية يقتضي تلافيها وتدارك أمرها بشيء يكون به استغفار لما اجترح، وتمحيص لما اكتسب، وذلك يكون بالرجوع إلى ذكره، والعود إلى خدمته والتضرع إليه، وإظهار تقواه، والطاعة له قولاً وعملاً، ليغفر له ما حصل منه، والصلاة هي النهاية في استجماع ذلك، ولذلك قال تعالى: وَأَقِمِ الصَّلَوةَ طَرَفِي النَّهَارِ وَزُلَفًا مِنَ الَّيْلِ إِنَّ الْحَسَنَتِ يُذْهِبْنَ السَّيِّئَاتِ) ولذلك لم تخل ملة من ملل الأنبياء عن الصلاة.
And the fourth: that the servant, in the times of his night and day, is not safe from minor sins and types of transgressions, and the requirement of servitude necessitates making up for them and rectifying their matter with something by which there is seeking forgiveness for what he has perpetrated and purification for what he has earned, and that is by returning to His remembrance, and returning to His service, and supplicating to Him, and showing His piety, and obedience to Him in word and deed, so that He may forgive him what has occurred from him, and the prayer is the ultimate in gathering all of that. Therefore, Allah Almighty said: 'And establish prayer at the two ends of the day and at the approaches of the night; indeed, good deeds remove bad deeds.' (Hud 11:114) And therefore, no religious community among the communities of the prophets was devoid of prayer.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth wisdom of prayer. The author argues that human beings inevitably commit sins and shortcomings. The requirement of servitude demands that these be remedied through repentance and purification. The prayer is the most comprehensive act that gathers all forms of repentance, such as remembering Allah, returning to His service, supplication, showing piety, and obedience. The author cites Quran 11:114 to support that good deeds remove bad deeds. He also notes that prayer was universal among all prophetic communities, indicating its essential role in repentance.

Misconception: Prayer is only a ritual obligation and does not have a role in removing sins or effecting repentance.
Correction: The passage clarifies that prayer is specifically designed to seek forgiveness and purify sins. It is the comprehensive act of repentance, as supported by Quran 11:114 and the universal practice of prophets.

Checkpoint

A) It fulfills a legal obligation only.
B) It serves as a means of seeking forgiveness and purification.
C) It is only a form of physical exercise.
D) It is optional and not required for repentance.

Key Takeaway
Prayer is the comprehensive act of repentance that removes sins through remembrance, supplication, and obedience, as affirmed by the Quran and the universal practice of prophets.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage admits you are never free from minor sins throughout your day and night. When did you last commit a minor sin and fail to immediately turn to Allah in prayer to remedy it? Will you commit to performing a prayer today with full consciousness of the components mentioned (remembrance, service, supplication, piety, obedience)?

The verse says 'good deeds remove bad deeds.' Think of a specific bad deed you did recently. What concrete good deed—especially a prayer—will you perform today to actively remove its effect? When exactly will you do it?

The Wisdom Behind the Numbers of Obligatory Prayers and Rak'ats
Author explains the divine wisdom behind the numbers of daily prayers and rak'ats.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational and symbolic wisdom behind why Allah obligated three daily prayers and assigned ten rak'ats to each set.

Core Idea
The numbers of prayers and rak'ats are not arbitrary but embody deep wisdom.

In this passage, the author delves into the philosophy behind the structure of the five daily prayers. He explains that the numbers three and ten are not random but the first numbers that possess complete sets (beginning, middle, end; contracts). This reveals that Islamic worship is built on wisdom and meaning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the author uses terms like 'first number' and 'first of the contracts'.
2
Understand the logic for three prayers
The author states that three is the first number with a beginning, middle, and end, thus it falls within the category of 'many'. Reflect on why 1 or 2 would not suffice for this reasoning.
3
Reflect on the night prayer evolution
The passage explains that night prayers originally were two plus recommended witr, then witr became emphasized to obligatory level, making three. Consider the parallelism with daytime prayers.
The Quranic verse at the end ties the alternation of night and day to remembrance and gratitude.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأوجب ثلاثًا منها في النهار إذ هي أول عدد يوجب له المتبدأ أو المنتهى والواسطة، فتكون داخلة في حد الكثير، وعدد ركعاتها العشرة التي هي أول العقود. وفي الليل وإن كان في الابتداء شرعت صلاتان وكان الوتر في حد الاستحباب، فقد ألحقت في الآخرة في التأكيد بالفريضة، فحصل عدد صلوات الليل ثلاثا وعدد ركعاتها أيضا عشراً كما في صلاة النهار فكان الليل والنهار خِلْفَةٌ لِمَنْ أَرَادَ أَن يَنَّكَرَ أَوْ أَرَادَ شُكُورًا
And He obligated three of them (prayers) during the daytime, as they are the first number for which there is a beginning, an end, and a middle, so they fall within the category of 'many'. And the number of their rak'ats is ten, which are the first of the contracts. And in the night, although at the beginning two prayers were prescribed and the witr was in the realm of recommendation, later it was annexed in emphasis to the obligatory, so the number of night prayers became three, and the number of their rak'ats also ten, as in the daytime prayer. So the night and day are 'alternating' (khilfah) for one who desires to remember or desires gratitude. (Quran 25:62)
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage discusses the number of obligatory prayers. The author states that Allah obligated three prayers during the daytime, and he provides a philosophical reason: the number three is the first number that has a beginning, middle, and end, thus it falls within the category of 'many' (al-kathir). The rak'ats of these three prayers total ten, which are the first of the 'contracts' (al-'uqud), possibly meaning the first complete set? Then for the night, originally only two prayers (maghrib and isha?) were prescribed, and witr was recommended, but later witr was made emphasized to the level of obligatory, so night prayers also became three, and their rak'ats also ten. He quotes Quran 25:62 about the alternation of night and day for remembrance and gratitude, implying the wisdom in this pairing.

Key Terms
الصلوات الخمس (five prayers)
الركعات (rak'ats)
الوتر (witr prayer)
الكثير (many)
العقود (contracts)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The number of daily prayers and rak'ats is arbitrary or based solely on convenience.
Correction: The author shows that the numbers are chosen with wisdom: three is the first complete number (beginning, middle, end) and ten is the first set of contracts, reflecting rational and symbolic order.

Checkpoint

A) Because three is the number of prophets.
B) Because three is the first number with a beginning, middle, and end, making it 'many'.
C) Because there are three times of day.
D) Because of a specific hadith.

Key Takeaway
The numbers of prayers and rak'ats reflect divine wisdom, balancing simplicity and completeness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that the number of daily prayers and rak'ats is not random but rooted in divine wisdom. When you perform your prayers, do you often think about this wisdom, or do you just go through the motions? How can you cultivate a deeper awareness of the order Allah has placed in your worship starting today?
?

Are you using each prayer as a moment to truly remember and thank Allah, or have they become routine breaks in your day? What will you change to ensure your prayers become genuine acts of gratitude?
?
The Witr prayer was initially optional but later given great emphasis. Is there any act of worship in your life that you have been treating as optional but now realize requires more commitment after understanding this lesson? What specific step will you take this week to strengthen that practice?

The Four Postures of Prayer as Humility
Enumerates four prayer postures as forms of humility with king analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the four postures of Islamic prayer symbolize the stages of approaching a great king in humility.

Core Idea
Prayer postures are a living parable of a servant approaching a king.

The author draws a powerful analogy between the physical acts of prayer and the progression of a humble servant entering the presence of a majestic king. This lesson unpacks that analogy, showing how each posture from standing to kneeling reflects a deeper spiritual reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the literal translation. Let the author's description of the postures and the king analogy sink in.
Notice the four postures are listed in order: standing, bowing, prostrating, kneeling.
2
Identify the four postures
List the four divisions of humility mentioned: standing, bending the back (bowing), rubbing the face on the ground (prostration), and kneeling on the knees.
These correspond to qiyam, ruku', sujud, and julus.
3
Understand the king analogy
The author compares the sequence of prayer to a servant approaching a great king: standing in awe, bowing when called near, prostrating in extreme humility, and kneeling when told to sit. Reflect on how each posture maps to a stage of approach.
This analogy highlights the spiritual significance behind the physical acts.
4
Reflect on uniqueness
Note the author's claim that no prayer in any other religion is performed in this manner. Consider why this specific combination of postures is unique to Islam.
The passage ends by hinting at the honor of the adhan.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[أنواع الخشوع والخضوع التي تجمعها الصلاة]: ثم هي استجمعت جميع أنواع التخاضع والتخاشع إذ هي أقسام أربعة: أحدها: القيام بين يديه. والثاني: تحنية الظهر له. والثالث: تعفير الوجه بالأرض. والرابع: الجثو على الركبتين. ثم شرع الدخول فيها بالقول والعمل وهما التكبير ورفع اليدين والخروج أيضا بهما، وهما الالتفات على الجانبين والتسليم، ثم المتحرم بها يأتي من أركانها بالشيء بعد الشيء على حال شبيهة بحال من تقدم إلى ملك عظيم فوقف بين يديه مستشعرا الهيبة مثنيًا عليه حتى إذا استدناه طامن له ظهره، ثم إذا زاد في الاستنداء عفر وجهه لفرط الخضوع بالأرض، حتى إذا أمره بالجلوس جثا على ركبتيه بين يديه ملازما في أشكاله الأربعة لإقامة حق الثناء والتمجيد على اسم المبالغة، ولن يوجد على هذه الهيئة صلاة في ملة من الملل، ولو ( ) لم يكن لها شرف إلا الأذان
[Types of humility and submissiveness that prayer gathers]: Then it gathered all types of humility and submissiveness, for they are four divisions: First: Standing before Him. Second: Bending the back to Him. Third: Rubbing the face on the ground. Fourth: Kneeling on the knees. Then entering into it was prescribed with speech and action, namely the takbir (saying Allahu Akbar) and raising the hands, and leaving it also with both, namely turning the head to both sides and the taslim (saying salam). Then the one who begins it performs its pillars one after another in a state resembling the state of one who approaches a great king: he stands before him, feeling awe, praising him until, when he brings him near, he bends his back for him; then when he increases in drawing near, he rubs his face on the ground out of extreme humility; then when he commands him to sit, he kneels on his knees before him, persisting in his four forms to fulfill the right of praise and glorification in the intensive form. And no prayer exists in this manner in any religious community. And if ( ) it had no honor except the call to prayer (adhan)...
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage describes the four postures of prayer as embodying all forms of humility. The author lists them: standing, bowing, prostrating (rubbing face on ground), and sitting on knees. Then he explains that the entrance and exit of prayer are also prescribed with specific actions: takbir and raising hands, and for exit, turning the head and taslim. He draws a beautiful analogy: the prayer's sequence mirrors a servant approaching a great king: standing in awe, then bowing when called near, then prostrating in extreme humility, then kneeling in his presence. This unique form of worship is not found in any other religion. He concludes by mentioning the honor of the adhan (call to prayer).

Key Terms
الخشوع (humility)
القيام (standing)
الركوع (bowing)
السجود (prostration)
الجثو (kneeling)
التكبير (takbir)
التسليم (taslim)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The prayer postures are mere physical exercises without symbolic meaning.
Correction: The author explicitly states that the postures represent stages of humility before God, likening them to a servant approaching a great king.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what do the four postures of prayer represent?
A) Different physical exercises.
B) Stages of approaching a great king.
C) Random movements.
D) Only a form of worship without meaning.

Key Takeaway
The physical postures of prayer are not mere movements; they are a profound expression of humility mirroring the servant's approach to a king.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Four Types of Humility in Prayer and Its Superiority
Enumeration of four postures of humility and exposition of prayer's unique virtues.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four postures of humility in Islamic prayer and the unique virtues that distinguish it from all other religions.

Core Idea
Islamic prayer is a unique embodiment of complete humility before Allah.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi's Tabsirat al-Adilla presents a vivid description of the physical postures of prayer and their symbolic meaning. The author argues that no other religion has a form of worship that combines such comprehensive humility and majesty. As you read, notice how the analogy to approaching a great king deepens the understanding of each posture.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Pay attention to the sequence of postures.
2
Identify the four types of humility
The passage enumerates four divisions of prayer that combine all types of humility. Find them and note how each relates to the analogy of approaching a king.
3
Understand the analogy to a great king
The author compares the worshiper to someone approaching a king, progressing from standing to kneeling. Reflect on how this illustrates increasing nearness to Allah.
This analogy is central to appreciating the prayer's depth.
4
Appreciate the unique virtues
The passage highlights the virtues of the call to prayer (adhan) and the Friday prayer (jumu'ah). Consider why these elevate Islamic prayer above others.
The author emphasizes that no other religion has such a combined virtue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم هي استجمعت جميع أنواع التخاضع والتخاشع إذ هي أقسام أربعة: أحدها: القيام بين يديه. والثاني: تحنية الظهر له. والثالث: تعفير الوجه بالأرض. والرابع: الجثو على الركبتين. ثم شرع الدخول فيها بالقول والعمل وهما التكبير ورفع اليدين والخروج أيضا بهما، وهما الالتفات على الجانبين والتسليم، ثم المتحرم بها يأتي من أركانها بالشيء بعد الشيء على حال شبيهة بحال من تقدم إلى ملك عظيم فوقف بين يديه مستشعرا الهيبة مثنيًا عليه حتى إذا استدناه طامن له ظهره، ثم إذا زاد في الاستنداء عفر وجهه لفرط الخضوع بالأرض، حتى إذا أمره بالجلوس جثا على ركبتيه بين يديه ملازما في أشكاله الأربعة لإقامة حق الثناء والتمجيد على اسم المبالغة، ولن يوجد على هذه الهيئة صلاة في ملة من الملل، ولو لم يكن لها شرف إلا الأذان المعلق حكمة برفع الصوت على المرفأة بالتكبيرتين والشهادتين والدعوة إلى المرغوبين الشريفين لكان شرفها عاليًا شرف كل صلاة لكل أمة من الأمم، ثم لو لم يكن لهم فيها إلا الجمعة المؤسسة في كل أسبوع، على أن سعى كل مملكة إلى سرتها ليجتمعوا في البقعة الواحدة ويخرج إليهم سائسهم بشعاره من أنواع الأسلحة، ويختص لنفسه مرتفعًا يشرف منه على رعيته، فيقبل إليهم بالوعظ والإرشاد والوعد والإيعاد، ويذكرهم مصالح داريهم، ثم يثنى بعد إخلاص الحمد لمولى النعم، وبعد الصلاة على أنبيائه عليهم السلام عموماً خاتمهم خصوصا وعلى الراشدين من خلفائه، وعلى جميع من يكون عليهم من أمرائه، ليشعر القلوب وتقوى النفوس على طاعتهم، والقوم مصغون إليه لا يجوز لأحد منهم أن يشتغل بشيء من الأحاديث والحركات عند التدبير لما يقرع أسماعهم، حتى إذا فرغ من خطبته يقدم جماعتهم ويقيم لطبقات رعاياه تلك العبادة المعظم قدرها لكان ذلك شرفًا لن يعقل جلال خطره إلا المتحقق لمجامع أركان الدين مع الملك، وليس لشيء من الأديان الأخر مثل هذه الفضيلة الرفيعة، والله الموفق.
Then it (prayer) gathered all types of humility and lowliness, for it is four divisions: First: standing before Him. Second: bending the back to Him. Third: rubbing the face on the ground. Fourth: kneeling on the knees. Then the entry into it was legislated by speech and action, which are the takbir and raising the hands, and also the exit by them, which are turning the face to both sides and the salutation. Then the one entering it (the prayer) performs its pillars one after another in a state similar to the state of one who advances to a great king and stands before him, feeling awe and praising him, until when he brings him near, he bows his back to him, then when he increases in nearness, he rubs his face on the ground due to extreme humility, until when he orders him to sit, he kneels on his knees before him, persisting in his four forms to fulfill the right of praise and glorification in the superlative sense. And no prayer exists in any religion in this form. And if it had no honor except the call to prayer attached to wisdom by raising the voice from the minaret with the two takbirs and the two testimonies and the call to the two noble desires, its honor would be higher than the honor of every prayer of every nation. Then, if they had nothing in it except the Friday prayer established every week, that each kingdom proceeds to its capital to gather in one place, and their ruler comes out to them with his emblem from types of weapons, and he designates for himself an elevated place from which he overlooks his subjects, then he turns to them with exhortation, guidance, promise and threat, and reminds them of the interests of their two abodes, then after sincere praise to the Lord of blessings and after prayers upon His prophets generally and their seal particularly and upon the rightly-guided among his successors and upon all of his commanders who are over them, to instill in hearts and strengthen souls in obedience to them, while the people listen to him, it is not permissible for any of them to occupy himself with any speech or movement at the time of the sermon, due to what strikes their ears, until when he finishes his sermon, he leads their congregation and establishes for the classes of his subjects that worship whose magnitude is magnified — then that would be an honor whose majestic rank is not comprehended except by one who realizes the comprehensive pillars of religion with sovereignty. And no other religion has anything like this lofty virtue. And Allah is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage is a classification and exposition of the virtues of Islamic prayer. The author first enumerates the four types of humility and lowliness that prayer combines: standing, bowing, prostrating (rubbing the face on the ground), and kneeling. He then describes the ritual of entering and exiting prayer (takbir, raising hands, turning the head, salutation). The author draws an analogy to approaching a great king: the supplicant stands in awe, bows when brought near, prostrates with extreme humility, and finally kneels. This sequence mirrors the increasing intimacy with the Divine. He asserts that no other religion has such a form of prayer. Even the call to prayer (adhan) alone would elevate Islamic prayer above others. He further highlights the unique features of the Friday prayer: the ruler emerges with his army, delivers a sermon, and leads the congregation, instilling obedience and reverence. The author concludes that this virtue is only comprehended by one who understands the pillars of religion together with political authority. This passage is a sustained argument for the unparalleled excellence of Islamic prayer.

خضوع

تحنية الظهر
تعفير الوجه

تسليم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The physical postures of prayer are merely empty rituals.
Correction: The author emphasizes that these postures embody comprehensive humility and lowliness before Allah, and the analogy to approaching a king shows their depth.

Checkpoint

B) 3
C) 4
D) 5

Key Takeaway
Islamic prayer uniquely combines four postures of humility with the virtues of the call to prayer and Friday gathering, unmatched by any other religion.

The author says no other religion has a prayer like ours. Do you treat your prayer as a unique privilege or a routine burden? What will you do tonight to remember its unmatched honor?
?
The Friday sermon involves the ruler guiding his subjects. Are you listening to the khutbah as if it comes directly from your King? What distraction will you eliminate this Friday to truly absorb the message?

The Wisdom and Benefits of Zakat
The author enumerates the wisdom and benefits of zakat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the multiple wisdom behind zakat, including its spiritual purification, social benefit of aiding the weak, and psychological training in generosity.

Core Idea
Zakat is not just a tax; it purifies the soul, aids the weak, and trains in generosity.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi explains why zakat is a divinely ordained duty rooted in wisdom. He moves beyond the simple notion of a financial obligation to show how it benefits the giver and the recipient, both in this world and the next. Let's explore the layered purposes of zakat as he presents them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation, noting the key terms like زكاة, تزكي, المعونة للضعفاء, رياضة النفس, and الشح.
Notice the structure: an opening statement followed by a list of benefits.
2
Identify the three main benefits
Find where the author mentions purification of the giver, assistance for the weak, and training the soul. The phrase 'المعونة للضعفاء' is central.
3
Reflect on the social dimension
Consider why the author says there is no benefit more comprehensive than the strong aiding the weak. This connects to gratitude to Allah for one's own wealth.
The social benefit is emphasized as the greatest.
4
Consider the psychological aspect
Understand how zakat trains the soul to fulfill trusts and abandon stinginess. The word 'التمسح' may be misread; it likely means 'generosity' (التسمح).
This shows zakat's role in character development.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الزكاة فقد ورد بها النبي له على ما تقتضيه الحكمة، إذ هي تزكي صاحبها وتثمر له الحمد والثناء، وفي إيجابها أعظم وجوه المصالح، وهو المعونة للضعفاء، ولا مصلحة أعم من أن يظهر القوى للضعيف حسن المعونة على تسوية حاله وأمر معاشه مع ما فيه من شكر الله تعالى على ما اختصه به من السعة، ورياضة النفس على أداء الأمانات، والتبرؤ من الشح والتمسح لأبواب
And as for zakat, the Prophet (peace be upon him) has legislated it according to what wisdom requires, for it purifies its owner and brings him praise and commendation. In its obligation are the greatest aspects of benefits, which is assistance for the weak. And there is no benefit more comprehensive than the strong showing the weak good assistance in setting right his condition and his livelihood, along with gratitude to Allah for what He has favored him with of abundance, and training the soul to fulfill trusts, and disavowing stinginess and wiping at doors.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage defines the wisdom and benefits of zakat. The author states that zakat is a divinely legislated duty based on wisdom. It purifies the giver (both spiritually and financially) and yields praise. The greatest benefit of its obligation is the assistance provided to the weak. The author emphasizes that there is no more comprehensive benefit than the strong aiding the weak to improve their situation, while also fostering gratitude to Allah for one's own wealth. Additionally, zakat trains the soul to fulfill trusts (amanat) and to abandon stinginess and adopt generosity. The passage ends abruptly, but it clearly presents zakat as a comprehensive social and spiritual institution.

Correction: The passage emphasizes that zakat purifies the giver, aids the weak, fosters gratitude, and trains the soul in generosity, showing it has spiritual, social, and psychological dimensions.

Key Takeaway

The Wisdom and Virtue of the Friday Sermon
Explains the wisdom behind the Friday sermon and its exclusive honor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the profound wisdom behind the structure of the Friday sermon and why it holds an honor unmatched by any other religious practice.

Core Idea
The Friday sermon is a divinely designed ceremony combining kingship and prophecy for spiritual and temporal guidance.

The Friday sermon is far more than a ritual – it is a majestic ceremony that mirrors a king's address to his subjects, yet with a divine purpose. In this passage, the author unpacks the wisdom behind each element: the raised voice, the takbirs, the testimonies, and the call to prayer and salvation. He then draws a vivid analogy to a worldly king's procession to highlight the sermon's unique honor. Let's explore how this practice elevates the Friday congregation above all others.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation together. Notice the flow: it starts with the sermon's components, then moves to the kingly analogy, then to the preacher's role, and finally the exclusive honor.
Pay attention to the terms 'takbiratayn' and 'shahadatayn'.
2
Identify the sermon's components
List the elements mentioned: raising the voice from the minaret, two takbirs, two testimonies, and the call to the two noble desires (prayer and salvation). Ask: Why are these specific elements highlighted?
3
Understand the kingly analogy
Analyze the comparison to a king proceeding to his capital, gathering subjects, ascending a high place, and delivering a speech. How does this analogy illustrate the sermon's honor?
The analogy emphasizes ceremony, authority, and the combination of worldly and religious interests.
4
Reflect on the exclusive claim
The passage concludes that no other religion has such an exalted virtue. Consider why the author makes this claim. What does this imply about the Friday sermon's role in Islam?
This connects to the learning objective about unmatched honor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المعلق حكمة برفع الصوت على المرفأة بالتكبيرتين والشهادتين والدعوة إلى المرغوبين الشريفين لكان شرفها عاليًا شرف كل صلاة لكل أمة من الأمم، ثم لو لم يكن لهم فيها إلا الجمعة المؤسسة في كل أسبوع، على أن سعى كل مملكة إلى سرتها ليجتمعوا في البقعة الواحدة ويخرج إليهم سائسهم بشعاره من أنواع الأسلحة، ويختص لنفسه مرتفعًا يشرف منه على رعيته، فيقبل إليهم بالوعظ والإرشاد والوعد والإيعاد، ويذكرهم مصالح داريهم، ثم يثني بعد إخلاص الحمد لمولى النعم، وبعد الصلاة على أنبيائه عليهم السلام عموماً خاتمهم خصوصا وعلى الراشدين من خلفائه، وعلى جميع من يكون عليهم من أمرائه، ليشعر القلوب وتقوى النفوس على طاعتهم، والقوم مصغون إليه لا يجوز لأحد منهم أن يشتغل بشيء من الأحاديث والحركات عند التدبير لما يقرع أسماعهم، حتى إذا فرغ من خطبته يقدم جماعتهم ويقيم لطبقات رعاياه تلك العبادة المعظم قدرها لكان ذلك شرفًا لن يعقل جلال خطره إلا المتحقق لمجامع أركان الدين مع الملك، وليس لشيء من الأديان الأخر مثل هذه الفضيلة الرفيعة، والله الموفق.
The preacher, by wisdom, raising his voice upon the minaret with the two takbirs and the two testimonies and the call to the two noble desires (prayer and salvation), its honor would be high above the honor of every prayer for every nation. Then even if they had nothing else in it except the Friday (prayer) established every week, considering that every king proceeds to his capital to gather in one place, and their ruler goes out to them with his insignia of various types of weapons, and he chooses for himself a high place from which he overlooks his subjects, then he turns to them with admonition, guidance, promise and threat, and reminds them of the interests of their two abodes (this world and the next). Then he praises after sincere praise to the Master of blessings, and after prayer upon His prophets, peace be upon them, generally, and their seal particularly, and upon the rightly guided caliphs, and upon all of his commanders over them, to instill awe in hearts and strengthen souls in obedience to them. And the people listen to him; none of them is allowed to occupy themselves with any talk or movements during the sermon because of what strikes their ears. Until when he finishes his sermon, he leads their congregation and establishes for the classes of his subjects that worship whose rank is magnified. That would be an honor whose greatness of standing is comprehended only by one who has realized the comprehensive pillars of religion along with kingship. And no other religion has such an exalted virtue. And God is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage elaborates on the profound wisdom and unparalleled honor of the Friday sermon (khuṭbah). The author begins by describing the sermon's elements: the preacher's voice from the minaret, the two takbīrs (shouting 'Allāhu akbar'), the two shahādatayn (testimonies of faith), and the call to the two noble desires—prayer and salvation. If these were the only merits, the sermon would already surpass all prayers of other nations. The author then draws a parallel to a worldly king who, with full ceremony, gathers his subjects in one place, appears with his regalia, ascends a high platform, and delivers an address combining admonition, guidance, promise, and warning about both worldly and otherworldly interests. After praising God and sending blessings on the prophets (especially the Seal) and the righteous caliphs and commanders, he expects silence and undivided attention from the audience. When the sermon concludes, he leads the congregational prayer. This elaborate ceremony is an honor whose grandeur is understood only by those who grasp the full scope of religion and kingship. The author emphasizes that no other religion possesses such an exalted virtue. The passage ends with 'And God is the granter of success.'

Key Terms
المعلق (المُلْقِي) (al-muʿalliq / al-mulqī)
المرفأة (al-marfaʾah)
التكبيرتين (al-takbīratayn)
الشهادتين (al-shahādatayn)
المرغوبين الشريفين (al-marghūbayn al-sharīfayn)
الجمعة (al-jumuʿah)
الخطبة (al-khuṭbah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the Friday sermon is merely a ritual with no deeper wisdom; it is just a routine talk before prayer.
Correction: This passage shows that the sermon is a divinely designed ceremony with profound wisdom: it combines elements of kingship and prophecy, involves specific verbal components (takbirs, testimonies), and is structured to inspire awe and provide comprehensive guidance for both worldly and religious life.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The Friday sermon is not merely a talk but a divinely ordained ceremony that mirrors royal majesty, combining spiritual and temporal authority to guide the community.

The Wisdom Behind the Obligation of Zakat
Explains the wisdoms and benefits of the obligation of zakat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the multiple wisdoms behind the obligation of zakat, including spiritual purification, social assistance, and moral training, as well as the Quranic support for these aspects.

Core Idea
Zakat purifies wealth and soul, assists the weak, and trains the soul in generosity.

The obligation of zakat is not merely a financial duty; it is laden with profound wisdom. In this passage, the author explains how zakat purifies the giver, provides for the needy, and trains the soul to overcome miserliness. We will explore these wisdoms and the Quranic verses that support them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to get an overall understanding of the author's argument.
2
Identify the wisdoms
Look for the various benefits mentioned: purification, praise, assistance to weak, training soul, etc.
Notice how the author connects each wisdom to the soul's purification and social good.
3
Examine the Quranic verses
The author quotes five verses to support the points. Match each verse to the wisdom it supports.
The verses are from Surah Al-Baqarah and At-Tawbah.
4
Reflect on the depth
Consider the author's final statement that the full wisdom is unfathomable. What does this teach about zakat?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الزكاة فقد ورد بها النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم على ما تقتضيه الحكمة، إذ هي تزكي صاحبها وتثمر له الحمد والثناء، وفي إيجابها أعظم وجوه المصالح، وهو المعونة للضعفاء، ولا مصلحة أعم من أن يظهر القوي للضعيف حسن المعونة على تسوية حاله وأمر معاشه مع ما فيه من شكر الله تعالى على ما اختصه به من السعة، ورياضة النفس على أداء الأمانات، والتبرؤ من الشح والتمسح لأبواب المصالح، إذا النفس مجبولة على الضن بالمال وشرف الإنسان مقرون بإفاضة الجود على وجوه الخيرات، ثم العقل يقتضي أن يتقرب العبد إلى خالقه بأداء ما هو الطيب دون الخبيث، وأن يتسارع إلى أداء ذلك بطيب نفس منه دون الكراهية والكسل، وأن لا يتوقع به الجزاء إلا من الله تعالى إذ هو يتقرب إليه ولا يمن على المعطي بما فعل، فورد ما بلغه محمد عليه السلام من الكتاب بذلك كله قال في الأول: يَأَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا أَنفِقُوا مِن طَيِّبَتِ مَا كَسَبْتُمْ وقال في الثاني: الَّذِينَ يُنفِقُونَ أَمْوَالَهُمُ ابْتِغاءَ مَرْضَاتِ اللَّهِ وَتَلْبِينَا مِنْ أَنفُسِهِمْ وقال في ذم من يضاد هذه الحالة: ﴿ وَلَا يَأْتُونَ الصَّلَوَة إِلَّا وَهُمْ كُسَالَى وَلَا يُنفِقُونَ إِلَّا وَهُمْ كَرِهُونَ ﴾ وقال في الثالث: لَا تُبْطِلُوا صَدَقَتِكُم بِالْمَنْ وَالْأَذَى وقال : يُؤْتِي مَالَهُ يَتَزَكَى يبين أصناف الأموال وما يجب في كل صنف، وأصناف من يجب الصرف إليهم على وجه لو استغرق الحكيم عمره بالتأمل ليقف على كنه ما في ذلك من الحكمة لما وقف عليه، والله الموفق.
And as for zakat, the Prophet (peace be upon him) brought it according to what wisdom requires, for it purifies its giver and produces for him praise and commendation. And in its obligation are the greatest aspects of interests, which is assistance to the weak. And there is no interest more general than that the strong shows to the weak good assistance in rectifying his condition and his livelihood, along with gratitude to God Almighty for what He has favored him with of abundance, and training the soul to fulfill trusts, and disassociating from miserliness and seeking the doors of benefits. For the soul is naturally inclined to stinginess with wealth, and the honor of man is linked to pouring generosity upon the faces of good deeds. Then reason dictates that the servant draw near to his Creator by giving what is good, not bad, and that he hasten to do that with a willing soul without dislike or laziness, and that he expect reward only from God Almighty since he is drawing near to Him, and not to bestow favors upon the recipient for what he did. So what Muhammad (peace be upon him) conveyed from the Book includes all that. He said in the first [verse]: 'O you who have believed, spend from the good things you have earned.' And He said in the second: 'Those who spend their wealth seeking the pleasure of God and from a firm assurance in their souls.' And He said in condemnation of those who oppose this state: 'And they do not come to prayer except while they are lazy, and they do not spend except while they are averse.' And He said in the third: 'Do not invalidate your charities with reproach and injury.' And He said: 'He who gives his wealth to purify himself.' He explains the kinds of wealth and what is obligatory upon each kind, and the kinds of those to whom it should be given, in a manner that if a wise man spent his entire life in contemplation to arrive at the essence of the wisdom in that, he would not arrive at it. And God is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage expounds the divine wisdom behind the obligation of zakat. The author begins by stating that the Prophet brought zakat as required by wisdom. Zakat purifies the giver (both wealth and soul) and brings him praise and commendation. Its obligation contains the greatest interests, primarily assistance to the weak. The most comprehensive interest is that the strong helps the weak to improve his condition and livelihood, while also fostering gratitude to God for wealth, training the soul to fulfill trusts, abandoning miserliness, and seeking charitable deeds. The author notes that human nature is avaricious, but honor is tied to generosity. Reason demands that a servant draw near to God by giving good things, willingly and eagerly, expecting reward only from God without imposing favors on recipients. The author then cites Qur'anic verses to support these points: 1) 'Spend from the good things you have earned' (Qur'an 2:267) — give what is good; 2) 'Those who spend their wealth seeking the pleasure of God and from a firm assurance in their souls' (Qur'an 2:265) — spending with sincere intention; 3) Condemnation of those who come to prayer lazily and spend reluctantly (Qur'an 9:54); 4) 'Do not invalidate your charities with reproach and injury' (Qur'an 2:264); 5) 'He who gives his wealth to purify himself' (Qur'an 92:18). Finally, the author states that the detailed rules of zakat regarding types of wealth, amounts, and recipients contain wisdom so profound that even a wise man spending his whole life in contemplation could not fully fathom it. The passage ends with 'And God is the granter of success.'

تزكي (tuzakkī)
الحكمة (al-ḥikmah)
المعونة للضعفاء (al-maʿūnah lil-ḍuʿafāʾ)
رياضة النفس (riyāḍat al-nafs)
الشح (al-shuḥḥ)
المن (al-man)
الأذى (al-adhā)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Zakat is merely a tax or financial obligation with no deeper significance.
Correction: The passage shows that zakat is a spiritual and moral training that purifies both wealth and soul, and is an act of worship with profound wisdom.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does zakat do for the giver?
A) It increases his wealth
B) It purifies him and produces praise for him
C) It makes him superior to the poor
D) It allows him to avoid charity

Key Takeaway
Zakat is an obligation with profound wisdom, purifying the giver, assisting the weak, and training the soul, supported by the Quran.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you give your zakat, do you reflect on its purpose of purifying your soul, or do you merely see it as a financial duty? What would need to change in your heart to make your giving a true act of worship that draws you closer to Allah?
?
The passage mentions that zakat trains the soul in generosity and fulfilling trusts, and that it should be given willingly without laziness. In which area of your life are you most reluctant to give—whether time, money, or effort? What concrete step will you take this week to give with a willing soul, seeking only Allah’s pleasure?
?
You are reminded that charity should not be followed by reproach (al-man) or injury (al-adhā). Think of a recent act of charity you performed: did you secretly feel proud or expect gratitude from the recipient? How can you ensure your giving remains pure and solely for Allah, without any trace of pride or expectation?

The Wisdom Behind Fasting: Abandoning Great Pleasures
Introduces the wisdom behind the obligation of fasting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the wisdom underlying the obligation of fasting as presented by Imam al-Nasafi: a deliberate abandonment of the greatest physical pleasures for spiritual discipline.

Core Idea
Fasting is not mere hunger: it is abandoning life's greatest pleasures for spiritual growth.

In this brief passage from the Tabsirat al-Adilla, Imam al-Nasafi opens the discussion on the wisdom behind the obligation of fasting. Rather than viewing fasting as a simple ritual, he presents it as a purposeful act of abandoning the very pleasures that sustain both body and lineage. This teaching prepares the reader to see fasting as a profound discipline, not just a physical ordeal.

The Wisdom of Jihad
Author explains why jihad was uniquely prescribed to Prophet Muhammad and its wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that jihad was uniquely prescribed to Prophet Muhammad for the purpose of guarding the religion and realm, and that its command transcends worldly benefits.

Core Idea
Jihad preserves the faith and community, not worldly gain.

In this passage, the author explains why jihad is unique to Islam and why it was prescribed. He argues that jihad serves as a protective measure for the religious community and its territory, without which enemies would cause widespread corruption. Additionally, he emphasizes that the command of jihad is rooted in divine wisdom, not worldly or political advantage.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to grasp the author's argument.
Notice the emphasis on 'no worldly benefit' in the command.
2
Identify the two purposes of jihad
The author states that jihad comprises guarding the religious community (al-millah) and the realm (al-baydah), and defending its sanctity. Also, the Prophet commanded it in a way that no worldly motive is involved.
3
Reflect on the consequence without jihad
The author describes what would happen without such defense: enemies would destroy, corruption would spread, and mosques would be demolished. This highlights the necessity of jihad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجهاد فهو عليه السلام مخصوص به، ولم يكن في دين من الأديان ما به وهو ما تقتضيه الحكمة البالغة إذ هو مشتمل على حراسة الملة والبيضة والذب عن حريمها، ولولا قيام أهل الدين بالمحاماة عن الدين والبيضة بالسيف، لاجتاحهم الأعداء، ولظهر الفساد في البر والبحر، ولهدمت المساجد، ثم هو عليه السلام أمر به على وجه عرف ذو البصيرة أن ليس وراءه مصلحة ولا يدانيه سياسة، ومن قرأ ما ذكره محمد بن الحسن في كتاب السير الكبير لعرف ذلك، ولا جنح لإطالة الكتاب بإيراد ذلك، والله المحمود.
As for jihad, he (peace be upon him) was specifically distinguished by it, and it did not exist in any other religion—that which [it contains] is what profound wisdom necessitates, for it comprises guarding the religious community, the realm, and defending its sanctity. Were it not for the people of religion defending the religion and the realm with the sword, the enemies would have destroyed them, corruption would have appeared on land and sea, and mosques would have been demolished. Then, he (peace be upon him) commanded it in a manner such that a person of insight knows that no worldly benefit lies behind it nor does any policy approximate it. Whoever reads what Muhammad ibn al-Hasan mentioned in the book al-Siyar al-Kabir will know that, and I do not incline to lengthen the book by citing that. And to God belongs all praise.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage defines jihad and explains its wisdom, noting that it is unique to the Prophet Muhammad and was not present in previous religions. He emphasizes that without the people of religion defending the religion and the realm with the sword, enemies would destroy them, corruption would spread on land and sea, and mosques would be demolished. The author then notes that the Prophet commanded jihad in a way that a person of insight knows there is no worldly benefit or political advantage behind it. He refers to Muhammad ibn al-Hasan's book al-Siyar al-Kabir for further evidence, but refrains from quoting it to avoid lengthening the book. The passage concludes with praise to God.
