The Four Aspects of Devotion in Hajj
The author explains the wisdom of Hajj and enumerates four aspects of devotion it embodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four aspects of devotion embodied in Hajj and the wisdom behind its rituals in humbling the proud.

Core Idea
Hajj transforms the pilgrim from pride to pure servitude through four acts of devotion.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi explores the profound wisdom of Hajj, revealing how its rituals are not merely physical acts but embody deep spiritual meanings. The author breaks down the worship into four key aspects that together cultivate complete servitude to God, while also showing how the rituals are designed to humble even the most powerful.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the full translation. Notice how the author first states the limitation of intellects in grasping the wisdom of Hajj.
The passage is dense—take your time.
2
Identify the four aspects of devotion
The author lists four divisions of worship in Hajj: ihram, tawaf, standing at Arafat, and the post-ritual state. Locate each one and understand what spiritual state it represents.
Each aspect is tied to a specific ritual.
3
Reflect on the wisdom behind the rituals
The author explains that Hajj is performed by those with strength, wealth, and status—qualities that can lead to pride. God made the rituals contrary to the ways of tyrants to instill humility and equality.
Consider why kissing a stone or throwing pebbles might be humbling.
4
Summarize the core idea
In your own words, explain how the four aspects of Hajj work together to transform a person from pride to sincere servitude to God.
Use the key terms like التقشف and الجبرية.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحج فقد ورد به النبي محمد عليه السلام، وفيه من الحكمة ما تقصر عنه العقول وإن وفرت الألباب وإن كملت، وجملة ذلك أن العبد ينتفع من مولاه بقدر الخدمة له والتعظيم لأمره والسعي في لوازمه، وجهة التعبد للمولى تنقسم إلى أربعة أقسام: أحدها إظهار التقشف والتشعث ورفض أسباب التزين وهجران أسباب الاستمتاع ليتصور بذلك على صورة المسخوط عليه المتعرض بما يتعاطاه من حاله لعطف سيده عليه، واستجلاب لرحمته إياه، وهذه المعاني كلها في الإحرام. والثاني: إتعاب بدنه بالجولان والطواف، وملازمة الأمكنة المنسوبة إلى مولاه ليتصور بصورة المقبل على خدمته الطائف بيابه اللائذ بحبله وعصمته، وهذا المعنى واقع في الطواف. والثالث: أعمال اللسان بالتعظيم له والثناء عليه والتزام التحميد والاستغفار والدعاء مع التضرع مستقبلاً عثرته مستوهباً جريرته، وهذا تحت الوقوف بعرفات. والرابع أن العبد إذا فعل تلك الثلاثة وفاز بحسن موعوده له بالرضا عنه والتجاوز عن خطاياه أن يسكن إليه، ويزيل عن نفسه التقشف وينقضي عن هيئته الشعث، ويعود إلى خدمته على صورة من نال عفو ملكه وسعد بمرضاته، فيعاود الزينة ويراجع الوضاءة، ويباسط مولاه بمسألة حوائجه، ويظهر افتقاره إليه ليكون قد قضى أمر العبودية وتمم حقوق نفسه في خدمة ربه، وواقع تحت الحج وتواعبه، ثم لما كان الحج عبادة لا يتوصل إلى أدائها إلا من له فضل قوة في الجسم وسعة في المال وبسطة في الحال والجاه – وهذه المعاني من دواعي الكبر والسطوة والجبرية - جعل الله تعالى هيئة أداء هذه العبادة على صورة مضادة لأحوال الجبابرة، وفي الطرف الأبعد من هيئات المتكبرين، وهو هجران الزينة رأسًا، والسعي في المواطن عدوا وتحريك الأطراف بلا سبب، ورمي الأحجار لا إلى أحد، وتقبيل حجر ولا ينفع، وتعظيم مواطن لا تجدي نفعاً ولا تمنع؛ ليعترف به الجبابرة لمر الخضوع ويذعن الأشراف لخالص العبودية، وتصير الأقدام سواسية في ذات الله تعالى، فمن نال في الدنيا ملكًا عظيمًا أو جاها وشرفًا وسعة في الأموال متى لابس عملاً لا يلائم صورة المتكبر به، وينافي أفعال الجبابرة رأسًا من الإقبال على حجر أصم بالتقبيل وعلى مواضع ميتة بالإجلال يصير ذلك أبلغ سبب في ارتفاض الكبر، وأتم رياضة لا يضر
As for Hajj, it was brought by the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him), and it contains wisdom that intellects fall short of, even if minds are abundant and complete. The summary of that is that the servant benefits from his Lord to the extent of his service to Him, magnification of His command, and striving in its requirements. The aspect of worship to the Lord is divided into four divisions: The first is showing austerity, dishevelment, abandoning the means of adornment, and forsaking the means of enjoyment, so that by that he appears in the form of one who is displeased with him, seeking through his state the compassion of his master and the drawing of his mercy. All these meanings are in ihram. The second: tiring his body by moving about and circumambulating, and staying in places attributed to his Lord, so that he appears in the form of one who approaches his service, circumambulating at his door, seeking refuge in his rope and protection. This meaning occurs in tawaf. The third: actions of the tongue in magnification of Him, praise of Him, commitment to praise, seeking forgiveness, and supplication with humility, acknowledging his fault and seeking forgiveness for his crime. This falls under standing at Arafat. The fourth: that when the servant does those three and attains the goodness of what was promised to him—pleasure with him and forgiveness for his sins—he should rest near Him, remove from himself austerity, shed his disheveled state, and return to His service in the form of one who has obtained his master's pardon and is blessed with His pleasure. He resumes adornment and returns to brightness, extends himself to his Lord by asking for his needs, and shows his neediness to Him, so that he has completed the matter of servitude and fulfilled the rights of his self in serving his Lord. This occurs under Hajj and its associated acts. Then, since Hajj is an act of worship that can only be performed by one who has additional bodily strength, wealth, ample circumstances, and status—and these things are causes of pride, arrogance, and tyranny—God Almighty made the manner of performing this worship in a form contrary to the states of tyrants and in the furthest extreme from the forms of the arrogant: abandoning adornment completely, walking swiftly in the sites, moving the limbs without reason, throwing stones at no one, kissing a stone that does not benefit, and magnifying places that bring no benefit and cannot prevent harm—so that tyrants recognize the bitterness of submission, nobles submit to pure servitude, and feet become equal in the essence of God Almighty. Whoever in this world attains great kingship or status, honor, and abundant wealth—when he engages in an action that does not suit the form of the arrogant and conflicts completely with the acts of tyrants, such as turning to a deaf stone to kiss it and to dead places to magnify them—that becomes the most effective cause for the lowering of pride and the most complete discipline that does not cause harm.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage explains the wisdom of Hajj and its four aspects of devotion. The author begins by stating that Hajj was prescribed by the Prophet and contains wisdom beyond the reach of intellects. He summarizes that the servant benefits from his Lord according to his service and magnification of God's command. The aspect of worship is divided into four divisions: First, ihram involves austerity, dishevelment, and abandoning adornment and enjoyment, so that the pilgrim appears like one seeking the master's compassion. Second, tawaf involves tiring the body by circumambulating and staying in sacred places, symbolizing the servant's approach to his Lord. Third, standing at Arafat involves actions of the tongue—praise, supplication, and confession of faults. Fourth, after completing these, the pilgrim removes austerity and returns to normal state, resuming adornment and asking for needs, indicating the completion of servitude. Then the author notes that Hajj is performed only by those with strength, wealth, and status—qualities that lead to pride—so God designed the rituals in a form contrary to tyrants and arrogant people: abandoning adornment, walking swiftly, throwing stones, kissing a stone, and magnifying places that bring no worldly benefit. This makes tyrants and nobles submit to pure servitude and establishes equality before God. The author concludes that such acts are the most effective means to lower pride and discipline the soul without harm.

Misconception & Correction

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
After completing the three stages of devotion (austerity, physical exertion, and verbal prayer), the pilgrim is to ‘resume adornment’ and ‘ask for needs.’ In your own life, after a period of intense worship or hardship, do you naturally return to gratitude and continued dependence on God, or do you fall back into self-reliance? What would it take for you to make that return a permanent state?

The Killed Dies by His Appointed Term
Nasafi states the Maturidi position on lifespans and the death of the killed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi position that the killed person dies by his decreed lifespan and how death and killing are distinguished.

Core Idea
Death is God's direct creation; killing is merely the human occasion.

This lesson introduces the Maturidi doctrine on lifespans. Nasafi affirms that even the killed person dies by God's appointed term, and carefully separates the act of killing (from the human) from the event of death (from God). This distinction is central to Maturidi theology of divine omnipotence and human accountability.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the Maturidi position
Focus on how Nasafi defines the relationship between killing and death. Note the key terms: 'ajal, mawt, qatl.
The passage begins 'الكلام في الآجال'.
2
Identify the two acts
Nasafi says killing is the killer's act subsisting in him, while death is what God creates in the killed. See how he uses 'فعل القاتل' and 'مفعول الله تعالى'.
3
Understand the role of custom
The phrase 'بطريق إجراء العادة' explains that God normally creates death after the killer's act, but the causal link is not necessary. This safeguards God's sole power over life.
This is a key Maturidi concept.
4
Reflect on the implications
Why does this matter? It resolves the problem of divine decree and human action: the killer is responsible for the act, but God creates the death by His decree.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في الآجال: المقتول عندنا ميت بأجله، وما يخلق الله فيه من المعنى المنافي للحياة قتلاً، كما يسمى مفعول الله تعالى وليس بفعل للقاتل وهو الموت، والقتل فعل القاتل قائم به ليس بحال في المقتول، والفعل الذي يوجد في القاتل ويوجد الله عقيبه انزهاق الروح أو الموت في المحل بطريق إجراء العادة يسمى ما يتفرق عقيبه جسم صلب كسرا، والتفرق مفعول الله تعالى لا صنع للعبد فيه، وزعم الكعبي أن المقتول غير ميت لأن الموت من فعل الله والقتل من فعل القاتل، وقال أجزاء
Discussion on lifespans: The killed person, according to us, dies by his appointed term. What God creates in him of the meaning contrary to life is killing, just as it is called the act of God, and it is not an act of the killer — and it is death. Killing is the act of the killer subsisting in him, not in the killed. The act which occurs in the killer and after which God creates the departure of the soul or death in the locus by way of the running of custom is called what occurs after it is a breaking of a solid body, and the breaking is an act of God, not an act of the servant. Al-Ka'bi claimed that the killed person is not dead because death is from God's act and killing is from the killer's act. And he said parts...
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on the decreed lifespans (ajāl). The author states the Maturidi position: the killed person indeed dies by his appointed term. He distinguishes between death (al-mawt) and killing (al-qatl). Death is what God creates in the killed — a meaning contrary to life — and it is God's act, not the killer's. Killing, on the other hand, is the act of the killer and subsists in the killer, not in the killed. The act that occurs in the killer, after which God creates the departure of the soul or death in the locus by the running of custom, is called 'breaking a solid body' (kasar). This breaking is God's act, not the servant's. The author then cites al-Ka'bi's opposing view: that the killed person is not dead because death is from God and killing from the killer. The passage breaks off with 'wa qāla ajzā’' (and he said parts), suggesting a further rebuttal not included here. The core doctrine is that while the killer performs the act of killing, the actual death is directly created by God, preserving God's sole power over life and death.

Key Terms
أجل
موت
قتل
مقتول
ميت
فعل
إجراء العادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The killer causes death independently of God's decree.
Correction: Nasafi clarifies that death is solely God's creation; the killer's act is merely the occasion by God's custom.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, what is the relationship between the killer's act and the death of the killed?
A) The killer directly creates death in the killed.
B) Death is God's creation, and the killer's act is merely the occasion by God's custom.
C) The killed person does not truly die.
D) Both the killer and God share in creating death.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi position affirms that even the killed person dies by God's decree, with death being God's direct creation and killing the human act that occasions it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that your appointed term is fixed by God alone, how would it change the way you think about protecting yourself from harm? Would you still take precautions? Where is your trust actually placed?
?
When you hear of someone killed unjustly, do you feel that their death was God's decree or an accident? How does this lesson challenge the way you console others or make sense of tragedy?
?
Think of a time you feared for your life. Did you truly believe that your death was entirely in God's hands, or did you act as if it depended on your own efforts? What would it mean to live with that conviction daily?

Refuting al-Ka'bi on Death and Killing
Nasafi presents and begins refuting al-Ka'bi's view on the death of the killed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ka'bi's objection and the Maturidi counter-argument that the killed does die by his term despite the distinction between death and killing.

Core Idea
Death and killing are distinct but inseparable in the decreed lifespan.

This lesson focuses on the specific objection raised by al-Ka'bi, who argued that the killed person is not dead because death is from God and killing from the killer. Nasafi's response, though cut short, reaffirms that the killed indeed dies by his term. We examine the underlying theological stakes of this debate.

Guided Path
1
Read Ka'bi's claim
Ka'bi asserts that because death and killing have different agents (God and human), the killed is not truly dead. Identify his reasoning.
His argument is based on a strict separation of acts.
2
Analyze the Maturidi response
Although the passage is cut off, the prior paragraphs already establish that death is created by God after the killer's act, so the killed does die by his term. Consider how Nasafi would refute Ka'bi.
The refutation likely relies on the 'running of custom' concept.
3
Understand the theological stakes
If Ka'bi were right, divine decree over lifespans would be compromised. This debate protects the Maturidi commitment to God's omnipotence and predestination.
4
Reflect on the incomplete text
The passage ends with 'wa qāla ajzā’', indicating more of Ka'bi's argument. Consider how the refutation might continue.
This highlights the need for additional sources.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في الآجال: المقتول عندنا ميت بأجله، وما يخلق الله فيه من المعنى المنافي للحياة قتلاً، كما يسمى مفعول الله تعالى وليس بفعل للقاتل وهو الموت، والقتل فعل القاتل قائم به ليس بحال في المقتول، والفعل الذي يوجد في القاتل ويوجد الله عقيبه انزهاق الروح أو الموت في المحل بطريق إجراء العادة يسمى ما يتفرق عقيبه جسم صلب كسرا، والتفرق مفعول الله تعالى لا صنع للعبد فيه، وزعم الكعبي أن المقتول غير ميت لأن الموت من فعل الله والقتل من فعل القاتل، وقال أجزاء
Discussion on lifespans: The killed person, according to us, dies by his appointed term. What God creates in him of the meaning contrary to life is killing, just as it is called the act of God, and it is not an act of the killer — and it is death. Killing is the act of the killer subsisting in him, not in the killed. The act which occurs in the killer and after which God creates the departure of the soul or death in the locus by way of the running of custom is called what occurs after it is a breaking of a solid body, and the breaking is an act of God, not an act of the servant. Al-Ka'bi claimed that the killed person is not dead because death is from God's act and killing is from the killer's act. And he said parts...
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on the decreed lifespans (ajāl). The author states the Maturidi position: the killed person indeed dies by his appointed term. He distinguishes between death (al-mawt) and killing (al-qatl). Death is what God creates in the killed — a meaning contrary to life — and it is God's act, not the killer's. Killing, on the other hand, is the act of the killer and subsists in the killer, not in the killed. The act that occurs in the killer, after which God creates the departure of the soul or death in the locus by the running of custom, is called 'breaking a solid body' (kasar). This breaking is God's act, not the servant's. The author then cites al-Ka'bi's opposing view: that the killed person is not dead because death is from God and killing from the killer. The passage breaks off with 'wa qāla ajzā’' (and he said parts), suggesting a further rebuttal not included here. The core doctrine is that while the killer performs the act of killing, the actual death is directly created by God, preserving God's sole power over life and death.

Key Terms
الكعبي
غير ميت
موت
قتل
فعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the killed does not die by God's decree because the killer directly causes death.
Correction: Maturidis hold that although killing is a human act, death is still directly created by God, so the killed dies by his appointed term.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ka'bi, why is the killed person not dead?
A) Because the killer prevents God from creating death.
B) Because death is from God and killing is from the killer, so they are separate events.
C) Because the killed person never truly dies.
D) Because death is a natural process, not God's act.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ka'bi's objection challenges Maturidi predestinarianism by separating the agents of death and killing, but the Maturidi response affirms that death still occurs by God's decree via the running of custom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that the exact moment of your death is fixed by Allah alone, even if it comes by a human hand? Does your daily life reflect that trust, or do you subtly act as if your life is in your own control?
?
When you hear of someone dying in an accident or act of violence, do you attribute their death to Allah's decree, or do you feel it was a random tragedy? How would your reaction change if you consistently saw every death as coming from Him?
?
In what ways do you compartmentalize your life, separating Allah's decree from your own choices? Identify one area where you can today realign your perspective to see that both the outcome and the means are from Him.

The Correct Interpretation of Fitna in Q. 5:41
Nasafi interprets 'fitna' and rejects Mu'tazila view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi interprets 'fitna' in Q. 5:41 as punishment and rejects the Mu'tazila view that it means obligation and testing.

Core Idea
Fitna in this verse means punishment, not obligation.

This lesson examines Nasafi's exegesis of Q. 5:41. He presents two possible meanings for 'fitna'—punishment or tribulation—and argues that it cannot mean obligation because the Prophet never found that distressing. The verse is used to affirm that Allah's will cannot be thwarted by human efforts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that Nasafi begins with the verse and then gives two interpretations.
The empty parentheses in the Arabic indicate a missing verse bracket.
2
Identify the two interpretations
Nasafi mentions that 'fitna' is said to mean punishment (عذابه) or tribulation by stoning and killing. Note that he rejects the third interpretation (obligation/testing) attributed to the Mu'tazila.
3
Understand the Prophet's role
Nasafi argues that if 'fitna' meant obligation, the Prophet would have been distressed and would have asked Allah not to oblige His servants, but he did not. This proves the interpretation is wrong.
This shows how the Sunnah can be used to interpret the Qur'an.
4
Reflect on the second verse
Nasafi cites another verse from the same surah: 'Those are the ones for whom Allah did not intend to purify their hearts.' This reinforces that Allah's will is decisive.
The Mu'tazila claim that Allah intended purification, but the verse says 'lam yurid' (He did not intend).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الله تعالى: ﴿ وَمَن يُرِدِ اللهُ فِتْنَتَهُ، فَلَن تَمْلِك لَهُ مِنَ اللَّهِ شَيْئًا () قيل: فتنته أي عذابه وقيل امتحانه بالرجم والقتل وإرادة ذلك بدون إرادة ما يستوجب به إرادة ظلم وتأويلهم أن قوله تعالى: "فتنته" أي تكليفه وامتحانه خطأ، لأن النبي عليه السلام ما كان يشق عليه ذلك، وما كان يسأل أن لا يكلف الله تعالى عباده وقال تعالى : ( أُولَٰئِكَ الَّذِينَ لَمْ يُرِدِ اللهُ أَن يُطْهَرَ قُلُوبَهُمْ ) (٢). وعند المعتزلة أراد ذلك وفي الآيات كثرة ، ولهم اعتراضات عليها فاسدة لا معنى للاشتغال بذلك لوقوف من له أدنى علم على بطلان تلك الاعتراضات، وقال تعالى: ﴿ فَلَوْ شَاءَ لَهَدَنكُمْ أَجْمَعِينَ ) (۱) وقال تعالى: وَلَوْ شَاءَ رَبُّكَ لَآمَنَ مَن فِي الْأَرْضِ كُلُهُمْ جَمِيعًا ) (٢) وقال: وَلَوْ شَاءَ اللهُ مَا أَشْتَرَكُوا ) (۳).
And Allah Almighty said: 'And whoever Allah wants to try (fitna) – then you will never possess for him anything against Allah.' It is said: his trial (fitna) means his punishment, and it is said: his tribulation by stoning and killing, and to will that without willing what necessitates it would be willing injustice. Their interpretation that His statement 'his trial' means his obligation and testing is wrong, because the Prophet, peace be upon him, was not distressed by that, nor did he ask that Allah not oblige His servants. And He said: 'Those are the ones for whom Allah did not intend to purify their hearts.' And with the Mu'tazila, He intended that, and there are many verses, and they have corrupt objections to them, with which there is no point in occupying oneself because anyone with the least knowledge knows the falsehood of those objections. And He said: 'If He willed, He would have guided you all.' And He said: 'And if your Lord willed, whoever is on the earth would have believed, all of them together.' And He said: 'And if Allah willed, they would not have associated partners.'
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage is an exegetical explanation. Nasafi begins with the verse 'And whoever Allah wants to try (fitna)' from Surah al-Ma'idah (5:41). He presents two possible interpretations: the first is that 'fitna' means punishment; the second is that it means tribulation through execution and stoning. He then argues that God's willing such punishment without willing what necessitates it (i.e., disbelief) would be willing injustice. He then rejects the interpretation of the Mu'tazila that 'fitna' means obligation and testing (taklīf wa imtiḥān). His proof is that the Prophet (peace be upon him) never found that distressing and never asked God not to oblige His servants. He then cites another verse: 'Those are the ones for whom Allah did not intend to purify their hearts' (Q. 5:41). He notes that the Mu'tazila claim that God did intend (i.e., He willed purification) but the verse clearly says 'lam yurid' (He did not intend). Nasafi dismisses their objections to these verses as worthless and well-known to be false. He then cites three verses that explicitly state that God's will is decisive: if He willed, He would have guided all; if He willed, all would believe; if He willed, they would not commit shirk. These verses support the Maturidi position that God's will is all-encompassing and that whatever occurs is by His will.

Key Terms
فتنة
تكليف
امتحان
مشيئة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some claim 'fitna' always means trial/testing.
Correction: Nasafi shows that in this context it means punishment, and the Prophet's reaction disproves the testing interpretation.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what does 'fitna' mean in Q. 5:41?
A) Moral trial and obligation.
B) Punishment.
C) Idolatry.
D) Misfortune.

Key Takeaway
Fitna in Q. 5:41 means punishment, not obligation, as shown by the Prophet's reaction and the subsequent verse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that Allah's commands are burdensome or oppressive? How does the Prophet's attitude toward obligation challenge your own feelings? Will you change your perspective today?
?
When you hear a verse like 'whoever Allah wills to try...', do you assume it means everyone is tested equally, or do you accept that Allah's will is sovereign and can exclude some from guidance? Are you willing to submit to the clear meanings taught by the scholars, or do you cling to your own reasoning?
?
The Mu'tazila twisted the meaning of this verse to fit their theology. Is there any area in your life where you interpret the Quran to suit your desires rather than seeking the truth? What step will you take tonight to correct that?

Verses Affirming Allah's Absolute Will
Nasafi cites three verses affirming Allah's absolute will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how three Qur'anic verses demonstrate that Allah's will is all-encompassing and that no one can act against His will.

Core Idea
Allah's will is absolute and decisive over all human actions.

This lesson presents three verses that Nasafi cites to reinforce the Maturidi position on divine will. These verses explicitly state that if Allah willed, He could guide everyone, make everyone believe, and prevent all idolatry. They serve as clear proof that nothing occurs outside His will.

Guided Path
1
Read the three verses
Read the Arabic quotes of the three verses in order. Each one presents a conditional statement about Allah's will.
These verses are frequently used by the Maturidis to argue for predestination.
2
Understand the common theme
All three verses share the structure 'If Allah willed, [something would happen]'. This implies that whatever does happen is by His will.
3
Reflect on refutation of free will
Consider how these verses undermine the Mu'tazila assertion that humans create their own actions. If Allah had willed, they would have been guided.

Source Text

And He said: 'And if Allah willed, they would not have associated partners.'

Refuting the Universal Scope of 'Except to Worship Me'
Al-Nasafi refutes the claim that the verse 'except to worship Me' proves God wills all to believe.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi refutes the claim that the verse 'إِلَّا لِيَعْبُدُونِ' proves that God wills all humans to believe and worship.

Core Idea
General verses can be specified by established proofs; exceptions prove the rule is not universal.

Al-Nasafi here addresses a common objection: doesn't the verse 'except to worship Me' mean all humans are commanded to worship? He concedes the meaning but shows that even this verse is not universal—children and the insane are already excluded. Therefore, unbelievers can also be excluded based on other proofs. This is a classic kalam argument about specification (takhsis).

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The verse 'except to worship Me' is often used to argue that God wills all humans to worship Him. Read the passage to see how al-Nasafi responds.
This objection is from the Qadariyya or others who claim God wants all to believe.
2
Notice the concession
Al-Nasafi concedes for argument's sake that the verse means worship. This is a common dialectical move to grant the opponent's premise for the sake of argument.
3
Identify the specification
He points out that children and the insane are already excluded from the verse. Why? Because they are not obligated to worship. So the verse is not universal.
This exclusion is agreed upon by consensus.
4
Apply the same logic
If those groups are excluded, then unbelievers can also be excluded based on the proofs already established in earlier discussions.
5
Consider the second verse
He briefly mentions the verse about messengers being obeyed by Allah's permission, stating it is similarly specified by the same proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أنا وإن سلمنا أن المراد من الآية العبادة إلا أن الآية خص منها الصغار والمجانين فيخص المتنازع منها وهم الكفرة لما ذكرنا من الدلائل، وبقيت الآية محمولة على من علم منهم الإيمان والعبادة على أن بعضا منها لو لم يكن مخصوصا بالإجماع لأمكن تخصيص المتنازع فيه لما أقمنا من الدلائل فكيف وقد خص في الآية بعض الجن والإنس ؟!. وكذا قوله تعالى: أَرْسَلْنَا مِن رَّسُولٍ إِلَّا لِيُطَاعَ بِإِذْنِ اللَّهِ أنه مخصوص بما أقمنا من الدلائل.
Moreover, even if we concede that what is meant by the verse is worship, yet the verse has been specified by excluding from it children and the insane; so that which is disputed, namely the unbelievers, should be specified from it due to the proofs we have mentioned. And the verse remains applicable to those among them whom it is known that they believe and worship. Moreover, if some part of it were not specified by consensus, then it would be possible to specify that which is disputed due to the proofs we have established. So how (is it not possible) when some of the jinn and humans have already been specified in the verse?! And likewise His statement: 'We did not send any messenger except to be obeyed by the permission of Allah' – that it is specified by what we have established of proofs.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection from the verse 'إِلَّا لِيَعْبُدُونِ' (except that they worship Me). The author concedes for argument's sake that the verse means worship. However, he points out that it is not universal: children and the insane are already excluded by consensus. Therefore, on the same principle, unbelievers can be excluded by other proofs. The verse then applies only to those who actually believe and worship. He also briefly mentions the verse about messengers being obeyed by Allah's permission and states it is similarly specified.

Key Terms
إِلَّا لِيَعْبُدُونِ
عبادة
مخصوص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'إِلَّا لِيَعْبُدُونِ' proves that God wants all humans to worship Him, therefore He wills belief from everyone.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that the verse is specified: children and the insane are excluded by consensus, so it does not apply to all humans. Unbelievers can similarly be excluded by other proofs.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, what groups are already excluded from the verse 'إِلَّا لِيَعْبُدُونِ'?
A) The believers
B) Children and the insane
C) The jinn
D) The rich

Key Takeaway
Even when a verse appears general, established proofs can specify its application, so it does not necessarily apply to all individuals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever clung to a general religious statement without considering its exceptions, and in doing so, judged others unfairly? How can you train yourself to seek the full context before making such judgments?
?
When you encounter a verse or hadith that seems to condemn a group, do you immediately apply it to people you dislike, or do you pause to consider if there are exceptions and proofs that limit its scope? What would it take for you to develop that pause?
?
If God's will is deeper and more nuanced than a single verse can capture, are you willing to submit to the complexity of His wisdom even when it challenges your simplistic understanding? What is one area where you need to let go of your own interpretation and trust the scholars who have reasoned with proofs?

Refuting the Ease Verse Argument
Al-Nasafi refutes the claim that the ease verse proves God does not will unbelief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi refutes the misuse of the ease verse to argue that God cannot will unbelief.

Core Idea
God's intended ease in the Quran refers to fasting concessions, not faith versus unbelief.

The ease verse is often cited to claim that God cannot will unbelief because it is hardship. Al-Nasafi shows that this interpretation ignores the verse's context and actual meaning. Let's dive into his response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the structure of the objection and answer.
Notice the opponent's use of the verse and al-Nasafi's two-part response.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent argues that since unbelief is the greatest hardship, and God wills ease, He cannot will unbelief.
This is a common misunderstanding of the verse.
3
Understand al-Nasafi's first point
Al-Nasafi notes that the verse is addressed to believers in the context of fasting (2:183-185).
The preceding verses specify the audience.
4
Grasp the second point
He explains that 'ease' means the concession to break fast, and 'hardship' means the difficulty of fasting, not unbelief.
Thus the verse has no bearing on God's will regarding unbelief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما تعلقهم بقوله تعالى: يُرِيدُ اللهُ بِكُمُ الْيُسْرَ وَلَا يُرِيدُ بِكُمُ الْعُسْرَ والكفر من أعسر العسر. فالجواب عنه: أن هذا خطاب للمؤمنين بقوله تعالى: يَأَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا كُتِبَ عَلَيْكُمُ الصِّيَامُ كَمَا كُتِبَ عَلَى الَّذِينَ مِن قَبْلِكُمْ ونحن نقول: لم يرد بهم الكفر. ثم التعلق في هذه المسألة بهذه الآية جهل، لأن الآية وردت في إثبات الرخصة للمسافر والمريض بالإفطار والقضاء في عدة من أيام أُخر، فكان المراد من اليسر هو الترفيه والرخصة لا الإيمان، فكان المراد من العسر ما يضاد الرخصة من التشديد والتضييق لا الكفر.
As for their attachment to His statement: 'Allah intends for you ease and does not intend for you hardship' – and unbelief is the hardest of hardship – the answer to it is: That this speech is addressed to the believers in His statement: 'O you who have believed, fasting is prescribed for you as it was prescribed for those before you.' And we say: He did not intend for them unbelief. Then attachment in this matter to this verse is ignorance, because the verse came to establish a concession for the traveler and the sick to break the fast and make up the days on other days. So what is meant by ease is relaxation and concession, not faith. And what is meant by hardship is what is contrary to concession, namely strictness and restriction, not unbelief.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage refutes an objection based on the verse 'يُرِيدُ اللهُ بِكُمُ الْيُسْرَ' (Allah intends ease for you). The opponent argues that since unbelief is the greatest hardship, God cannot intend it. Al-Nasafi responds: the verse is addressed to believers in the context of fasting concessions (2:183-185). The 'ease' refers to the permission to break the fast when traveling or ill, not to faith. Likewise, 'hardship' refers to the difficulty of fasting in those conditions, not to unbelief. Therefore, the objection is invalid.

Key Terms
يُرِيدُ اللهُ بِكُمُ الْيُسْرَ
رخصة
كفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, to whom is the ease verse directly addressed?
A) All humanity
B) The believers
C) The polytheists
D) The Jews and Christians

Key Takeaway
The ease verse cannot be used to argue that God does not will unbelief because it specifically addresses fasting concessions for believers, not a general principle about faith.

?
How does knowing that the 'ease' verse is about fasting concessions change the way you recite it or rely on it in your daily life? Will you commit to studying the context of every verse you use in argument?

Refuting al-Ka'bi's Hadith Argument on Patience
Refutes al-Ka'bi's argument from the hadith on patience.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the hadith 'Whoever is not pleased with My decree...' does not support al-Ka'bi's position on divine decree and sin.

Core Idea
The hadith applies to calamities, not to disbelief or eternal punishment.

Al-Ka'bi used a famous hadith to argue that being pleased with divine decree means accepting even sins. But is that what the hadith really means? In this passage, the author carefully dissects the Prophet's words, showing that they refer to illnesses and misfortunes, not to matters of faith. He then turns the argument back on al-Ka'bi, exposing a contradiction in Mu'tazilite thought.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and al-Ka'bi's argument
Start by reading the full Arabic passage. Notice how al-Ka'bi quotes the hadith and the author’s immediate response.
The hadith is central to the debate.
2
Identify the author's main claim
The author states that the hadith’s true meaning concerns illnesses and calamities, not divine decrees about belief or disbelief.
This is the key distinction.
3
Examine the counter-argument
The author points out that according to Mu'tazilite theology, eternity in Hell is decreed by Allah. So if al-Ka'bi applies the hadith universally, he must accept that decree as well.
This reveals an inconsistency.
4
Understand the distinction between disbelief and affliction
The author explains that a disbeliever is pleased with his disbelief and does not want to leave it, while individuals are distressed by illnesses and calamities. The hadith addresses the latter.
This refutes al-Ka'bi's blanket application.
5
Reflect on the Mu'tazilite position
The author notes that Mu'tazilites are not pleased with afflictions without compensation, so they themselves fail the hadith’s test.
This turns the argument back on them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج الكعبي بقول النبي عليه السلام: من لم يرض بقضائي ولم يصبر على بلائي فليطلب ربا سواي. وقد بينا أنا رضينا بقضاء الله تعالى وأعلمنا كيفيته على أن حقيقة الخبر في الأمراض والمصائب ألا ترى أن الخلود في النار من قضائه عند المعتزلة إذ لا يثبتون الله تعالى تخليدًا سوى معنى الخلود، فليرض به الكعبي لنفسه، وإلا فليطلب ربا سواه يحققه أن الكفر عندنا وإن كان بقضاء الله تعالى إلا أن من قضى عليه ذلك یرضی به ويتمسك به تمسكًا لا يرضى بالزوال عنه، وإنما الذي لا يرضى به المقضي عليه ويضجر منه في الأحايين هو الأمراض والمصائب، والمعتزلة لا يرضون بها إلا بعوض فليطلبوا ربًّا سوى من قضى بها عليهم
And al-Ka'bi argued with the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him: 'Whoever is not pleased with My decree and does not have patience with My affliction, let him seek a lord other than Me.' And we have explained that we are pleased with the decree of Allah the Exalted and we have made known its modality, and that the true meaning of the report concerns illnesses and calamities. Do you not see that eternity in the Fire is from His decree according to the Mu'tazila, since they do not affirm for Allah the Exalted any eternity other than the meaning of eternity? So let al-Ka'bi be pleased with it for himself, or else let him seek a lord other than Him. The verification of this is that disbelief, according to us, even though it is by the decree of Allah the Exalted, yet the one upon whom it is decreed is pleased with it and adheres to it with an adherence that does not desire to depart from it. Rather, that which the one decreed upon is not pleased with and is at times distressed by are illnesses and calamities. And the Mu'tazila are not pleased with them except with compensation; so let them seek a lord other than the One who decreed them upon them.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage refutes al-Ka'bi's argument from the hadith: 'Whoever is not pleased with My decree and does not have patience with My affliction, let him seek a lord other than Me.' The author points out that the hadith refers to illnesses and calamities (amrad wa masa'ib), not to matters of belief such as eternal punishment. The Mu'tazila themselves, according to their own doctrine, believe that eternity in the Fire is decreed by Allah, yet they are pleased with it? No — actually they are not pleased with it. The author argues that a person who has been decreed disbelief is pleased with it and does not want to abandon it, so the hadith does not apply to him. It applies to those who are distressed by illnesses and calamities, and the Mu'tazila demand compensation for such afflictions, indicating they are not truly patient. Therefore, the hadith actually refutes al-Ka'bi's position.

Key Terms
قضائي
بلائي
رضا
خلود في النار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Applying the hadith to disapproval of divine decree regarding sins or disbelief.
Correction: The hadith specifically refers to illnesses and calamities, not to matters of belief or sin. Disbelievers are pleased with their disbelief, so the hadith does not apply to them.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the proper referent of the hadith 'Whoever is not pleased with My decree...'?
A) Eternal punishment in Hell.
B) Illnesses and calamities.
C) The decree of disbelief for some people.
D) The issue of free will.

Key Takeaway
The hadith about being pleased with divine decree applies to afflictions, not to sins or disbelief, and the Mu'tazilites' own stance contradicts their use of it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If you were to face a long-term trial, would you still be able to say you are pleased with Allah's decree? What would need to change in your heart to reach that level?
?
Are there any sins or shortcomings in your life that you have become comfortable with, like the disbeliever is pleased with his disbelief? How does the author's distinction challenge you to not confuse acceptance of sin with patience in affliction?

The Inconsistency of al-Ka'bi on Qadr
Criticizes al-Ka'bi for ignoring two hadiths on qadr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two key hadiths on qadr and why al-Ka'bi's selective acceptance is inconsistent.

Core Idea
Rejecting any part of qadr contradicts the prophetic tradition.

This passage confronts a common tendency: selectively accepting religious proofs while ignoring others. Al-Ka'bi used one hadith about affliction but overlooked two widely transmitted hadiths that firmly establish belief in qadr as essential. The author highlights this inconsistency to show that faith in divine decree is not optional—it is a foundational element of belief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two hadiths quoted and the criticism of al-Ka'bi.
2
Identify the two hadiths
Find the first hadith: 'Qadr, its good and its evil is from Allah.' Then the second hadith from Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas listing four things that make a believer.
The second hadith explicitly includes faith in qadr as one of the four.
3
Understand the critique
The author says al-Ka'bi took what agreed with his desire and left what contradicted it. Reflect on why this is a serious inconsistency.
This shows that rejecting qadr amounts to disbelief according to the hadith.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this passage defends the necessity of believing in qadr (its good and evil) as part of Islamic faith.
The hadith warns that concealing belief in qadr leads to disbelief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم العجب من الكعبي حيث سمع هذا الحديث فأخذ به ولم يسمع ما استفاض واشتهر وهو قوله عليه السلام: 'القدر خيره وشره من الله' وحديث سعد بن أبي وقاص عن النبي عليه السلام أنه قال: أربع من كن فيه فهو مؤمن ومن جاء بثلاث وكتم واحدة فقد كفر، شهادة أن لا إله إلا الله وأني رسول الله، وأنه مبعوث من بعد الموت، وإيمان بالقدر خيره وشره فمن جاء بثلاث وكتم واحدة فقد كفر لكنه رجل يأخذ ما وافق هواه ويترك ما خالف ذلك
Then the wonder is from al-Ka'bi that he heard this hadith and took it, but did not hear what has become widespread and famous, which is his saying, peace be upon him: 'Qadr (divine predestination), its good and its evil, is from Allah,' and the hadith of Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas from the Prophet, peace be upon him, that he said: 'Four things, whoever has them is a believer, and whoever comes with three and conceals one has disbelieved: testimony that there is no god but Allah and that I am the Messenger of Allah, and that he is resurrected after death, and faith in qadr, its good and its evil. So whoever comes with three and conceals one has disbelieved.' But he is a man who takes what agrees with his desire and leaves what contradicts that.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage criticizes al-Ka'bi for selectively accepting hadiths. He used the hadith about patience with affliction, but ignored two other well-known hadiths: first, the hadith 'Qadr, its good and its evil, is from Allah'; second, the hadith of Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas enumerating four things that make one a believer, one of which is belief in qadr. The author points out that al-Ka'bi is inconsistent: he accepts what suits his opinion (what agrees with his desire) and rejects what contradicts it. The second hadith explicitly states that denying belief in qadr (i.e., concealing it) amounts to disbelief. This is a strong argument against those who reject predestination.

Key Terms
القدر خيره وشره
إيمان بالقدر
أربع من كن فيه فهو مؤمن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Belief in qadr (divine decree) is optional or secondary to other beliefs.
Correction: The hadith of Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas places faith in qadr alongside the testimony of faith and belief in resurrection, and warns that concealing it leads to disbelief.

Checkpoint
According to the hadith of Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas cited in this passage, what is the status of someone who believes in three of the four things but conceals belief in qadr?
A) He is still a believer but deficient.
B) He is a hypocrite.
C) He is a disbeliever (kafir).
D) He is excused because of his ignorance.

Key Takeaway
Belief in qadr, its good and evil, is an essential part of faith, and denying it is tantamount to disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your life have you, like al-Ka'bi, accepted only what feels comfortable from Islam while ignoring teachings that challenge you? What are you willing to change starting today?
?
When something bad happens to you, do you truly believe it is from Allah with the same certainty as when good happens? How does your reaction reveal the state of your faith in qadr?
?
The hadith warns that concealing belief in qadr (its good and evil) is a form of disbelief. Have you ever downplayed or kept silent about this belief to avoid conflict or judgment? Will you commit to openly affirming it from now on?

Mu'tazilite Explanations for Children's Suffering
Nasafi presents the Mu'tazilite objection on children's suffering and their two answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite dilemma concerning children's suffering and the two answers they proposed.

Core Idea
Children's suffering challenges Mu'tazilite theology; they justify it through adult lessons or future compensation.

This passage confronts Mu'tazilite theology with a pressing problem: if God must always act for the greatest benefit, why do innocent children suffer? The Mu'tazilites propose two answers, each revealing tensions within their own principles. As you read, notice how the author refutes the first answer and begins to evaluate the second.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite principle and the objection
First, read the opening lines that restate the Mu'tazilite principle that God cannot favor anyone unless it is for their religious benefit. Then the objection is raised: children suffer without any sin or religious benefit.
The heading '[تألم الأطفال لماذا]?' signals the problem.
2
Identify the first answer
The first group of Mu'tazilites claims that children are pained so that adults (parents, witnesses) may take a lesson. Read this answer carefully.
This answer tries to shift the benefit from the child to others.
3
Understand the refutation of the first answer
The author refutes this by arguing that the child is harmed in this world, gains nothing in the afterlife, and that causing harm for another's benefit contradicts Mu'tazilite conditions of justice and wisdom.
The refutation has two main points: it is not beneficial for the child (thus unjust), and it makes God go against wisdom.
4
Consider the second answer
The majority of Mu'tazilites claim that the child's suffering is itself beneficial because God will compensate him with eternal reward. The passage cuts off, but the argument continues that without compensation, God would be unjust.
This answer appeals to future compensation to justify present suffering.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لما كان من مذهبهم أن ليس الله تعالى أن يختص برحمته ونعمته وتوفيقه وإصلاحه من يشاء بل الواجب عليه أن يسوي بين كل من عباده في ذلك إلا ما استحقه أحد بطاعته، فكذلك ليس له أن يخص واحدًا منهم بما لم يفعل بغيره مثله إلا إذا كان ذلك أصلح له في الدين أو استحقه بمعصيته فأما لا على ذلك الوجه فهو محال . [تألم الأطفال لماذا]؟ فقيل لهم : إن الأطفال يتألمون ويتوجعون وتصيبهم الأمراض والأوجاع، ولم يستوجبوا شيئًا من ذلك بصنيعهم لما لا تصور للجريمة منهم وليس ذلك أصلح لهم في الدين إذ لا تكليف عليهم فتفرقت المعتزلة في الاعتراض على هذا. فزعمت طائفة منهم أنهم يؤلمون ليتعظ بذلك الآباء والأمهات ومن عاين ذلك من البالغين. قيل لهم : إن كان ذلك للبالغين أصلح فلا شك أن هذا الطفل يتضرر به في الدنيا، ولا ينتفع به في الآخرة لما لا يصير ذلك داعيًا له إلى فعل طاعة أو الامتناع عن معصية لانعدام عقله للحال وعدم تصور تذكره لذلك عند البلوغ، أو لأنه ربما يموت قبل جري التكليف عليه وإثبات الأصلح لغير الصغير مما مفسدة للصغير تناقض منكم لوجهين: أحدهما : أن ما فعل بالصغير ليس بأصلح له، فإذا فعل بشخص ما ليس بأصلح له فيكون هذا في حقه ظلما وجورًا عندكم. والآخر أن عندكم من شرط الأصلح أن لا يصير بإعطاء الأصلح خارجًا عن الحكمة، وفي هذا خروجه عن الحكمة. وزعم جمهورهم أن هذا أصلح لهذا الصغير، لأنه يعوضه بذلك ثوابًا مخلدًا في الآخرة ولا يجوز أن يعوضه ولو لم يعوضه لكان ظالما، والإيلام لمنفعة
Then, since it is part of their doctrine that God Almighty is not to single out with His mercy, grace, success, and rectification whomever He wills, but rather it is obligatory upon Him to be equal between all of His servants in that except what one deserves by his obedience, so similarly He is not to single out any one of them with what He has not done for another like him unless that is more beneficial for him in religion or he deserves it by his disobedience. As for not on that basis, it is impossible. [Why do children suffer]? So it was said to them: Children experience pain, suffer, and diseases and aches afflict them, and they have not deserved any of that by their own actions because no crime is conceivable from them, and that is not more beneficial for them in religion since there is no obligation upon them. Then the Mu'tazilites divided in objecting to this. So a group among them claimed that they are pained in order for the fathers, mothers, and those adults who witness that to take a lesson. It was said to them: If that is more beneficial for the adults, there is no doubt that this child is harmed by it in this world, and does not benefit from it in the Hereafter because that does not become a motive for him to perform obedience or refrain from disobedience due to the absence of his intellect at present and the lack of conception of his remembering that at puberty, or because he may die before the imposition of obligation upon him. And establishing benefit for someone other than the minor is a cause of harm for the minor, which is a contradiction from you in two ways: One of them is that what is done to the minor is not more beneficial for him, and if something is done to a person that is not more beneficial for him, then that is injustice and tyranny according to you. The other is that according to you, one of the conditions of benefit is that giving the benefit should not cause the giver to go outside wisdom, and in this, it causes him to go outside wisdom. And the majority of them claimed that this is more beneficial for this minor, because he compensates him for it with an everlasting reward in the Hereafter, and it is not permissible for him not to compensate him, for if he did not compensate him, he would be unjust, and the infliction of pain for a benefit...
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage presents a problem for Mu'tazilite theology: if God must treat all equally unless it is for the benefit of the individual, how can children suffer undeservedly? The author first restates the Mu'tazilite principle that God cannot favor anyone unless it is for their religious benefit or due to their sin. Then he raises the objection: children suffer pain and disease without having committed any sin, and since they are not obligated (mukallaf), it cannot be for their religious benefit. The Mu'tazilites themselves are divided in response. The first group claims that children suffer to teach adults a lesson. The author refutes this: the child is harmed in this world and gains nothing in the Hereafter because he cannot understand the lesson due to lacking intellect, and he may die before reaching puberty. Moreover, causing harm to the child for the benefit of others contradicts the Mu'tazilite conditions of benefit: it is not beneficial for the child, so it is unjust, and it causes God to act outside wisdom. The second group (the majority) claims that the child's suffering is itself beneficial because God will compensate him with eternal reward in the Hereafter. The text cuts off here, but the argument continues that without compensation, God would be unjust. This passage is a combination of an objection and two Mu'tazilite answers, with a refutation of the first answer.

Key Terms
الأطفال
التألم
الأصلح
التكليف
التعويض
الإيلام
الحكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Children's suffering is always a punishment for sin or a test of their own faith.
Correction: The Mu'tazilites argue that children's suffering cannot be punishment (no sin) or a test (no obligation), so they propose it is for the benefit of others or compensated in the afterlife.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first Mu'tazilite answer to the problem of children's suffering?
A) Children are born sinful and deserve punishment.
B) Children are pained so that adults may learn a lesson.
C) Children are compensated with eternal reward.
D) Children's suffering is a test of their parents' faith.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites struggle to reconcile children's suffering with their doctrine of obligatory benefit, proposing either adult edification or future compensation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness a child suffering, does your heart instinctively question God's justice? How does the Mu'tazilite struggle in this passage mirror your own attempt to make sense of such pain? What would it mean for you to trust God's wisdom even when you cannot see the benefit?
?
Imagine you are the parent of a child who suffers a painful illness. Would the explanation that it teaches you a lesson or that your child will be compensated in the afterlife truly satisfy you? Why or why not? What does your answer reveal about your expectations of God?
?
This passage shows the Mu'tazilites trying to defend God's justice by imposing human conditions on Him. Are there ways you subconsciously place conditions on God—expecting Him to behave fairly according to your standards? Will you today surrender that expectation and submit to His absolute wisdom, even in the face of inexplicable suffering?

Infinite Regress in Paradise Refuted
Nasafi refutes infinite regress of reward and thanks in Paradise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite logic of reward and gratitude leads to an infinite cycle that contradicts the nature of Paradise as a place of grace, not obligation.

Core Idea
Mu'tazilite reasoning turns Paradise into a place of endless duty.

Nasafi here delivers a devastating reductio ad absurdum against the Mu'tazilite position on reward. If every reward demands gratitude, and gratitude demands another reward, then Paradise becomes an endless cycle of testing—contradicting the consensus that Paradise is a home of final rest and grace. This passage exposes the logical flaw in treating divine reward as a contractual obligation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the conditional structure and the harsh conclusion.
The passage uses a sharp rhetorical style.
2
Identify the absurd consequence
The passage shows that if reward obligates thanks, and thanks obligates new reward, then Paradise becomes a place of endless obligation. Identify each step in this chain.
3
Understand the consensus argument
Nasafi then cites consensus that Paradise is not a place of obligation (taklīf). Reflect on why this consensus is crucial to the refutation.
4
Reflect on the theological point
Contrast the Mu'tazilite view of reward as obligatory compensation with the Sunni view of reward as pure grace. Ask yourself: why does the infinite regress show the Sunni view is correct?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لو جاز هذا لجاز أن يقال بأن أهل الجنة إذا وصل إليهم الثواب يجب عليهم أداء شكره ، ثم إذا أدوا ذلك يجب على الله أن يثيبهم فيكون أهل الجنة أبدًا ممتحنين بأداء شكر ما أسدي إليهم من الثواب الذي استحقوه بأفعالهم. وهذا باطل بإجماع المسلمين، لما أن الجنة ليست بدار تكليف، وإذا كان الأمر على ما بينا علم أن ثواب الله تعالى بفضل منه وإنعام فينبغي أن تكون الجنة منغصة على قضية كلام هذا التائه في دينه المتحير في عقيدته، وذلك يوجب الخروج عن الدين. نعوذ بالله من ذلك.
Then, if this were permissible, it would be permissible to say that when the inhabitants of Paradise receive reward, they must offer its thanks; then when they do so, Allah must reward them again, so that the inhabitants of Paradise would always be tested in performing thanks for the reward that they deserved by their deeds. And this is false by the consensus of the Muslims, because Paradise is not a place of obligation (taklīf). Since the matter is as we have explained, it is known that Allah's reward is by His grace and favor. Therefore, Paradise should be tainted, according to the statement of this strayer in his religion, confused in his creed, and that necessitates leaving the religion. We seek refuge with Allah from that.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage draws out an absurd consequence of the Mu'tazilite position. If, as they claim, a reward creates an obligation to thank, and that thanks creates a new obligation for God to reward again, then the inhabitants of Paradise would be locked in an infinite cycle of obligation and testing. Nasafi states that this is invalid by the consensus of Muslims, because Paradise is not a house of obligation (taklīf); it is a house of recompense and enjoyment, not further duty. Therefore, the Mu'tazilite logic leads to the conclusion that Paradise would be a place of testing, which contradicts its purpose. He then harshly condemns the Mu'tazilite view as leading one out of the religion. The core argument: reward is pure grace, not an obligatory compensation.

Key Terms

شكر
تكليف
جنة
فضل
إجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That gratitude in Paradise is a required duty that God must reward.
Correction: Paradise is not a place of obligation (taklīf); gratitude there is natural, not a legal duty that creates a new claim on God. Reward is pure grace.

Checkpoint
Why does Nasafi say that the Mu'tazilite view would turn Paradise into a testing ground?
A) Because inhabitants would have to earn their rewards.
B) Because they would be locked in an infinite cycle of reward and thanks.
C) Because they would never be satisfied.
D) Because they would have to perform good deeds.

Key Takeaway
Mu'tazilite logic forces an infinite regress of reward and thanks, proving that Paradise reward is pure grace, not contractual compensation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that your good deeds put God in your debt? How does the teaching that reward is pure grace challenge that feeling, and what would it look like to truly believe that every blessing is unearned favor?
?
If Paradise is not a place of obligation, but of pure grace, what does that mean for how you eagerly anticipate eternal life? Does your heart long for a place of endless duty or endless gift?
?
When you reflect on your life, do you find yourself performing acts of worship with a sense of entitlement? How would your prayer, charity, and fasting change if you approached them as responses to grace rather than demands for wages?

The Absurdity of Requiring Compensation for Reward
Refutes the idea that God must compensate for reward, using the example of Paradise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that God must compensate for reward leads to an absurd conclusion, confirming that His reward is pure grace.

Core Idea
Reward in Paradise is not compensation; it is pure grace.

The author presents a logical refutation of the claim that God must compensate for reward. He demonstrates that if this principle were true, it would lead to an absurdity: the people of Paradise would be eternally tested with thankfulness and further reward. Since Paradise is not a place of obligation, the original claim is false. This passage shows that God's reward is pure grace, not a necessary compensation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage once in Arabic, then read the translation. Notice the structure: 'then if this were permissible...' The author sets up a conditional.
The passage uses a reductio ad absurdum.
2
Trace the logical argument
Identify the premise (the opponent's claim), the consequence (eternal testing in Paradise), and the refutation (consensus that Paradise is not a place of obligation). Write these in your own words.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that reward is by favor and grace. Ask yourself: why does this matter for Islamic theology?
This refutes the idea that humans can obligate God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لو جاز هذا لجاز أن يقال بأن (۱) أهل الجنة إذا وصل إليهم الثواب يجب عليهم أداء شكره ، ثم إذا أدوا ذلك يجب على الله أن يثيبهم فيكون أهل الجنة أبدًا ممتحنين بأداء شكر ما أسدي إليهم من الثواب الذي استحقوه بأفعالهم. وهذا باطل بإجماع المسلمين، لما أن الجنة ليست بدار تكليف، وإذا كان الأمر على ما بينا علم أن ثواب الله تعالى بفضل منه وإنعام فينبغي أن تكون الجنة منغصة على قضية كلام هذا التائه في (٢) دينه المتحير في عقيدته، وذلك يوجب الخروج عن الدين نعوذ بالله من ذلك.
Then if this were permissible, it would be permissible to say that the people of Paradise, when reward reaches them, must perform thanks for it. Then if they perform that, God must reward them, so the people of Paradise would be forever tested by performing thanks for the reward that was given to them, which they deserved by their deeds. And this is false by consensus of the Muslims, because Paradise is not a place of obligation. And if the matter is as we have clarified, it is known that the reward of God, the Exalted, is by His favor and bounty, so Paradise would be sullied according to the statement of this wanderer in his religion, confused in his creed, and that leads to exiting the religion—we seek refuge in God from that.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that God is obligated to compensate for reward. The author argues by analogy: if the opponent's principle—that any benefit must be compensated—were applied consistently, it would lead to an absurd consequence. Specifically, when the people of Paradise receive their reward, they would be obligated to thank God for it. Then, if they thank Him, God would be required to reward them again for that thanks, leading to an infinite regress of thankfulness and reward. This would make Paradise a place of continuous testing and obligation, which contradicts the Muslim consensus that Paradise is not a place of taklif (obligation). Therefore, the original claim is false. The author concludes that the reward of God is purely by His favor and grace, not as a necessary compensation. The passage ends with a strong condemnation of anyone who holds such a view, stating it leads to leaving the religion and seeking refuge in God.

Key Terms
ثواب
تكليف
فضل
إنعام
إجماع المسلمين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's reward is a necessary compensation for human deeds.
Correction: God's reward is purely by His favor and grace, not a required compensation.

Checkpoint
What is the absurd consequence if God were obligated to compensate for reward, according to the author?
A) People would never enter Paradise.
B) The people of Paradise would be eternally tested with thankfulness and further reward.
C) God would become unjust.
D) Children would be punished without cause.

Key Takeaway
God's reward in Paradise is not a compensation for deeds but a pure gift of His grace.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform good deeds, do you ever feel that God 'owes' you reward? How does this passage challenge that sense of entitlement and call you to see every blessing as pure grace?
?
If Paradise is a place where no obligation exists, what does that imply about how you should approach your acts of worship in this life—are you performing them as a debt to God or as a response to His unearned favor?
?
Imagine yourself standing in Paradise, receiving reward. Would your first thought be gratitude or a sense of deserving? What does your instinct reveal about your heart's true belief regarding God's grace?

False Views on the Punishment of the Grave
Nasafi presents the denial and two erroneous solutions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the denial of punishment of the grave by the Jahmiyya and Mu'tazila, and the two erroneous views proposed to explain it.

Core Idea
False solutions to the punishment of the grave deny the necessity of life.

The punishment of the grave is a firmly established doctrine in Sunni Islam, but it was challenged by the Jahmiyya and some Mu'tazila who argued that a dead body cannot be punished. In this passage, Imam Nasafi first recounts their objection, then presents two alternative views put forward by others to circumvent the objection — both of which he ultimately rejects. This lesson introduces these views and their refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage along with its translation to understand the flow of the argument.
Notice the initial objection and the two alternative proposals.
2
Identify the two false views
The passage presents two opinions: that of Abu al-Husayn al-Salihi and the Karamiyya, and that of Ibn al-Rawandi. Note how each tries to explain punishment without life.
3
Understand the refutation
The author concludes both views are false. Ask why he considers them unacceptable.
The refutation is brief; the next lesson will give the correct view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال يا رسول الله فإذا أكفيكهما والأخبار في الباب كثيرة. وأنكرت الجهمية وبعض المعتزلة ذلك لما أن تعذيب من لا حياة له والسؤال منه والجواب منه مستحيل والجواب عنه أن على أصل أبي الحسين الصالحي والكرامية يعذب ولا حياة له إذ ليست الحياة عندهم شرط لثبوت العلم على ما قررنا في أول مسألة وعند ابن الراوندي الحياة موجودة في كل ميت لأن الموت عنده ليس معنى مضاد للحياة بل هو آفة كلية معجزة عن الأفعال الاختيارية فلم تكن منافية للعلم. وهذه الأقاويل باطلة والصحيح أن الحياة شرط للعلم ولا تألم ولا تلذذ بدون العلم إلا أن الدلائل السمعية وردت بثبوت عذاب القبر فلابد من القول بثبوته ثم هو من الممكنات إذ الله تعالى أن يعيد إليه نوع حياة مقدار ما يتألم ويتلذذ فيعذبه، ويدل عليه ما روينا من حديث عمر الله حيث قال: أو يكون معي عقلي؟ ولا وجود للعقل بدون الحياة، وكذا يدل عليه قوله تعالى: (رَبَّنَا أَمَتَنَا اثْنَيْنِ وَأَحْيِيتَنَا اثْنَتَيْنِ) ثم من الجائز أن يعيد روح الحياة إليه فيحيى ومن الجائز أن يثبت فيه نوع حياة بدون إعادة الروح إليه، والتوقف لأصحابنا في كيفية عذاب القبر في هذا الحرف أن الحياة تثبت بدون إعادة روح الحياة أو مع إعادة روح الحياة فأما لا توقف لهم في أن لا يتصور التعذيب بدون الحياة إنما ذلك مذهب الصالحي والكرامية، وقد نص على اشتراط الحياة عندنا الشيخ أبو الحسن الرستغفني وشيخنا أبو منصور - رحمهما الله نص أيضا على اشتراط ذلك، ونص على أن التوقف في إعادة الروح وعدم إعادتها.
He said: 'O Messenger of God, then I suffice you both?' And reports on this matter are many. The Jahmiyyah and some of the Mu'tazila denied that, because torturing one who has no life, questioning him, and his answering are impossible. The answer is that according to the principle of Abu al-Husayn al-Salihi and the Karamiyya, he is tortured without life, since life is not a condition for knowledge according to them, as we established in the first question. And according to Ibn al-Rawandi, life exists in every dead person, because death in his view is not a meaning opposite to life, but rather a universal defect preventing voluntary actions, so it does not negate knowledge. These sayings are false. The correct view is that life is a condition for knowledge, and there is no pain or pleasure without knowledge. However, the scriptural proofs have come confirming the punishment of the grave, so it is necessary to affirm it. Then it is among possible things, for God Almighty can restore to him a type of life sufficient for pain and pleasure, and thus punish him. This is supported by what we narrated from the hadith of Umar (may God be pleased with him) where he said: 'Will my intellect be with me?' And there is no existence of intellect without life. Likewise, it is supported by His saying: 'Our Lord, You have caused us to die twice and given us life twice' (Quran 40:11). Then it is possible that He restores the spirit of life to him and he lives, and it is possible that He establishes in him a type of life without restoring the spirit to him. Our companions suspend judgment on the modality of the punishment of the grave regarding whether life is established without restoring the spirit of life or with restoring the spirit of life. However, there is no suspension for them that punishment cannot be conceived without life; that is only the doctrine of al-Salihi and the Karamiyya. And the condition of life with us was explicitly stated by Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Rustughfani, and our Shaykh Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on them) also explicitly stated its condition, and clearly stated that the suspension is regarding the restoration of the spirit or not.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage addresses the denial of the punishment of the grave by the Jahmiyya and some Mu'tazila. They argued that a dead person has no life, so torture, questioning, and answering are impossible. The author first reports two alternative views: (1) Abu al-Husayn al-Salihi and the Karamiyya hold that punishment can occur without life because life is not a condition for knowledge (and thus sensation) in their view; (2) Ibn al-Rawandi holds that life persists in every dead person because death is not the opposite of life but a lack of voluntary action. The author rejects both views as false. The correct position, he states, is that life is indeed a condition for knowledge and sensation, but the scriptural evidences (al-dalāʾil al-samʿiyya) overwhelmingly affirm the punishment of the grave. Therefore, it must be affirmed. Moreover, it is within God's power to restore a temporary life of sufficient duration for the deceased to experience pain and pleasure. He supports this with a hadith where Umar asks about his intellect, showing the link between life and intellect, and the Quranic verse 'You have caused us to die twice and given us life twice', indicating the possibility of multiple restorations of life. The author clarifies that the majority of his school (ashābunā) suspend judgment on the modality of punishment — whether life is restored without the spirit or with the spirit — but they do not suspend on the necessity of life itself for punishment. The contrary view is only held by al-Salihi and the Karamiyya. Finally, he cites the explicit statements of two early Māturīdī authorities, Abu al-Hasan al-Rustughfani and Abu Mansur al-Māturīdī, confirming that life is a necessary condition for the punishment of the grave.

Key Terms
عذاب القبر
حياة
علم
تألم
تلذذ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the dead cannot experience any sensation, so punishment of the grave is impossible.
Correction: While life is necessary for sensation, God can grant a temporary life to the deceased, making punishment possible.

Checkpoint
Which group denied the punishment of the grave according to this passage?
A) The Jahmiyya and some Mu'tazila
B) The Karamiyya and Ibn al-Rawandi
C) The Ash'aris and Maturidis
D) The Salaf

Key Takeaway
False solutions to the punishment of the grave involve denying the necessity of life, which contradicts sound reason and revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear about the punishment of the grave, does your heart accept it as a reality, or do you find yourself rationalizing it away like the Jahmiyyah? What does your reaction reveal about your iman in the unseen?
?
The false views tried to solve the 'problem' of punishing a lifeless body. But are you willing to submit to Allah's power even when it seems impossible to your reason? In what area of your life are you demanding that Allah's commands 'make sense' before you obey?
?
If you truly believed that the dead are punished or rewarded based on their deeds, would you change anything in your life today? What is holding you back from that change?

The Correct Position: Life is Necessary for Punishment
Nasafi states the correct view and provides evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct Sunni position that life is a condition for the punishment of the grave, and how scriptural evidence and rational possibility support it.

Core Idea
Punishment of the grave requires temporary life, which God can bestow.

After refuting the false views, Imam Nasafi now establishes the correct position. He affirms that life is necessary for sensation, but the overwhelming scriptural proofs (dalā'il sam'iyya) compel us to affirm punishment of the grave. Moreover, it is rationally possible for God to restore a minimal life to the deceased. He also clarifies a point of ambiguity among his school regarding the modality of that life.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note how the author builds his argument step by step.
2
Identify the principle
The author states that life is a condition for knowledge and sensation. This is the foundational principle.
3
Examine the scriptural proofs
The author cites a hadith of Umar and a Quranic verse as evidence. Understand how they support the necessity of life.
4
Understand the modality suspension
The author explains that his school suspends judgment on whether life is restored with or without the spirit, but not on the necessity of life itself.

Quranic Evidence for Life After Death for Martyrs
Quranic proof that martyrs are given life for reward.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Quran provides explicit evidence that martyrs are given a real life to experience reward, supporting the broader principle that the dead can be granted life for blessing or punishment.

Core Idea
Martyrs are alive with their Lord, experiencing reward—a literal life given by Allah.

This lesson examines two Quranic verses that demonstrate how Allah grants life to the dead for the purpose of experiencing pleasure or pain. The verses about martyrs serve as a proof that life can be present in the dead without being perceptible to us, affirming the reality of life after death in a non-ordinary sense.

Guided Path
1
Read the heading and verses
Start by reading the heading which states the purpose: to show that the dead are given life to feel pleasure or pain. Then read the two verses from Surah Al-Imran and Surah Al-Baqarah.
Notice that the heading sets the theological framework for interpreting these verses.
2
Identify the proof structure
The author presents two scriptural proofs. Identify how each verse directly supports the claim that the dead (specifically martyrs) are alive.
3
Reflect on the nature of this life
Consider that the verses mention 'alive with their Lord, receiving provision, rejoicing' and 'alive but you do not perceive.' This indicates a real life, not merely spiritual or metaphorical.
This counters the misconception that the life of martyrs is only symbolic.
4
Connect to broader doctrine
Understand that this evidence supports the earlier argument that life can exist in the dead for the purpose of reward or punishment, which is relevant to discussions on the barzakh and the torment of the grave.

Source Text
