Introduction to Names and Rulings for Grave Sinners
Introduction to the topic of names and rulings for grave sinners.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that a central theological debate concerns how to name and judge grave sinners from the Muslim community (ahl al-qibla) in the Hereafter.

Core Idea
The fate of grave sinners is a defining issue in Islamic theology.

The author transitions to a new discussion about the names and rulings (asma' wa ahkam) pertaining to major sinners among the people of the qibla. This introductory sentence sets up the debate on the intermediate status of grave sinners, a key topic that distinguishes Sunni theology from other sects like the Khawarij and Mu'tazila.

Guided Path
1
Read the introductory sentence
Read the sentence carefully: 'الكلام في الأسماء والأحكام والوعد والوعيد اختلف الناس في مقترفي الكبائر من أهل القبلة أنهم بماذا يسمون وأن حكمهم في الآخرة ما هو؟'
Identify the key terms: names, rulings, promise, threat.
2
Identify the subject of disagreement
The passage states that people differed about the perpetrators of major sins (kaba'ir) from among the people of the qibla (those who pray towards Mecca, i.e., Muslims).
This disagreement centers on what to call them and what their final fate will be.
3
Understand the significance
This question is crucial because it affects whether grave sinners remain believers or become disbelievers, and whether they will enter paradise or hell eternally.
The Sunni position distinguishes between sin and kufr.
4
Prepare for further exploration
This lesson sets the stage for detailed discussions on the names and rulings that will follow in subsequent lessons.

The Maturidi Principle for Eternal Threat Verses
Sanusi states the principle and applies it to the killing verse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidis interpret verses of eternal threat by restricting them to those who deem the sin permissible (istihlal), applying this to the verse on killing a believer.

Core Idea
Eternal threat verses only target those who deem the sin permissible, not ordinary believers.

Sanusi begins by laying down a key Maturidi principle: any verse of threat that mentions eternity (khulūd) actually refers to those who consider the sin lawful (mustaḥillūn), because their act of deeming it permissible is itself disbelief. He then applies this to the famous verse about killing a believer (4:93), arguing that its threat of eternal hell is only for the one who kills due to the victim's faith, not for a believer who kills without istihlal. This lesson unpacks that principle and its proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the principle statement
Read the first sentence carefully: 'والأصل عندنا أن ما كان من الآيات...'. This is the foundational claim. The principle is that threat verses conjoined with eternity are about the mustaḥillūn.
Notice the phrase 'مقرونا بذكر الخلود' — this is the key condition.
2
Identify the reasoning
Sanusi explains why: the threat is actually for disbelief (kufr) which occurs through istihlal, and the sin is mentioned only as a cause.
The crime is 'سببًا للكفر وطريقاً إليه'.
3
See the application to the killing verse
He applies this to Qur'an 4:93: 'وَمَن يَقْتُلْ مُؤْمِنًا مُتَعَمِّدًا فَجَزَاؤُهُ جَهَنَّمُ خَلِدًا فِيهَا'. He interprets 'intentionally' as killing for the sake of faith, which constitutes disbelief.
He contrasts this with intentional killing without istihlal, which only incurs qisas.
4
Refute the objection
An objection is raised: the threat seems to be for killing, not disbelief. Sanusi responds by showing that the punishment matches the mustaḥill, proving it is for kufr.
He states that eternal punishment for a believer is impossible (bāṭil).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأصل عندنا أن ما كان من الآيات الواردة في الوعيد مقرونا بذكر الخلود فهو من المستحلّين لذلك، لما أنهم كفروا لاستحلال ذلك فأوعدوا على كفرهم في الحقيقة وذكر تلك الجريمة لكونها سببًا للكفر وطريقاً إليه، ولهذا قلنا في تأويل قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَمَن يَقْتُلْ مُؤْمِنًا مُتَعَمِّدًا فَجَزَاؤُهُ جَهَنَّمُ خَلِدًا فِيهَا ﴾ أي متعمدًا الإماتة أي قتله لأجل أنه مؤمن ومن هذا قصده في القتل يكون كافرًا ألا ترى أنه حيث ذكر قتل العمد لا على الاستحلال فمن باب إيجاب القصاص بقي اسم الإيمان والأخوة بينه وبين المؤمنين وجعله أملاً للرحمة والتخفيف على ما بينا، فكان ذلك دليلاً أن هذه الآية وردت في المستحل وما يزعمون أن هذا وعيد القتل لا وعيد الكفر لأنه تعالى قابله بقتل المؤمن دون الكافر، والاستحلال كفر. قلنا هذا وعيد الكفر والله قابله بقتل المستحل لما أنه به يكفر فيكون جعل جزاء الكفر بمقابلته دليلاً على أنه كفر وإعلاما به على أنه لا وجه إلى جعله جزاء للقتل بدون الكفر لما أنه مؤمن وثبت ذلك بالدليل، والقول بخلود المؤمن في النار باطل فلا يمكنهم القول بأن هذا جزاء قتله إلا بعد إثبات زوال إيمانه وجعله في منزلة بين منزلتين أو القول بكفره والقول به باطل على ما قررنا، فدل أنه ليس بجزاء للقتل المطلق بل هو جزاء للقتل بطريق الاستحلال الذي هو كفر، وأما ما ليس بمقرون بذكر الخلود فمن الجائز أن يكون فى المؤمن إذ تعذيب المؤمن بقدر ذنبه ليس بمستحيل في العقل ولا قام دليل شرعي على امتناعه، ووردت به أخبار كثيرة ثم ثبت استغفار الملائكة والأنبياء عليهم السلام للمؤمنين، وكذا المؤمنون أمروا بالاستغفار بعضهم لبعض والاستغفار لمن لا يجز تعذيبه سؤال ترك الظلم وهذا كفر، وكذا مغفرة من لم يستوجب العذاب محال وكذا العفو دل أن من العباد من يستوجب العقوبة ثم الله تعالى يغفر له بفضله ويعفو عنه. يحققه أنه تعالى غافر الذنب، وكذا يسمى غفورًا وغفارا، ورد بهذه الأسامي نصوص الكتاب، وكذا الله تعالى عفو وتسمية الله تعالى بما لا يتحقق منه ومدحه نفسه بما لا يتصور منه محال، ثم بالإجماع ليس ذلك في حق المكذبين ولا الخارجين عن الإسلام، فدل أن ذلك في حق من ارتكب المعصية من غير الاستحلال فكان في دليل أن العبد بالمعصية لا يكفر وبالكبيرة لا يخرج عن الإيمان ليتحقق عفوه والمغفرة له ودليل جواز مغفرة ما دون الكفر إذ لولا ذلك لما تحقق
And the principle with us is that whatever verses have come down in threat conjoined with the mention of eternity (khulūd), they are [interpreted as referring to] those who deem [the sin] permissible (mustaḥillūn), because they disbelieved due to their deeming it permissible, so they were threatened on account of their disbelief in reality, and that crime is mentioned because it is a cause for disbelief and a path to it. For this reason we said in the interpretation of His statement, the Exalted: 'And whoever kills a believer intentionally, his recompense is Hell, abiding therein forever' – meaning intentionally doing away [with life], i.e., killing him because he is a believer, and whoever has this intention in killing becomes a disbeliever. Do you not see that when He mentioned intentional killing not on [the basis of] deeming permissible, it falls under the obligation of retaliation (qiṣāṣ), the name of faith and brotherhood remained between him and the believers, and He made him hopeful for mercy and leniency as we have explained? So that was evidence that this verse was revealed concerning the one who deems [it] permissible. And what they claim that this is a threat for killing, not a threat for disbelief, because He, Exalted, matched it with killing a believer, not a disbeliever – and deeming permissible is disbelief. We said: This is a threat for disbelief, and Allah matched it with killing the one who deems permissible because he disbelieves thereby, so making the recompense of disbelief correspond to it is evidence that it is disbelief and an announcement of it, and that there is no justification for making it a recompense for killing without disbelief because he is a believer, and that is established by evidence. The statement that a believer abides eternally in the Fire is false, so they cannot say that this is the recompense for his killing except after establishing the removal of his faith and placing him in a station between two stations, or saying that he is a disbeliever – and saying that is false according to what we have established. So it indicates that it is not a recompense for absolute killing, but rather a recompense for killing by way of deeming permissible, which is disbelief. As for what is not conjoined with the mention of eternity, it is possible that it applies to the believer, because punishing the believer according to his sin is not impossible in reason, nor has any legal proof (dalīl sharʿī) been established to prevent it, and many reports (akhbār) have come about it. Then it is established that the angels and the prophets, peace be upon them, seek forgiveness (istighfār) for the believers, and likewise the believers are commanded to seek forgiveness for one another. Seeking forgiveness for one who does not deserve punishment is asking for the abandonment of injustice, and this is disbelief. Likewise, forgiveness (maghfira) for one who does not deserve punishment is impossible, and likewise pardon (ʿafw) indicates that among the servants are those who deserve punishment, then Allah the Exalted forgives him by His bounty and pardons him. Confirming this: He, Exalted, is the Forgiver of sin (ghāfir al-dhanb), and likewise He is named Ghafūr and Ghaffār; the texts of the Book have come with these names. And likewise Allah the Exalted is ʿAfūw. Naming Allah with what is not actualized in Him, and His praising Himself with what is not conceivable from Him, is impossible. Then by consensus (ijmāʿ), that does not apply to the deniers (mukadhdhibīn) nor to those who have left Islam. So it indicates that that applies to the one who committed disobedience without deeming it permissible. Thus it was an evidence that the servant does not disbelieve by disobedience and does not leave faith by a major sin, so that His pardon and forgiveness may be actualized for him. And evidence for the permissibility of forgiving what is less than disbelief, for if not for that, it would not be actualized.
Source pages p. 394

Commentary
This passage expounds the Maturidi position on interpreting verses of threat (āyāt al-waʿīd) that include the mention of eternity (khulūd). The author begins by stating the fundamental principle (al-aṣl ʿindanā): any threat verse that is conjoined with the mention of eternity refers specifically to those who deem the sin permissible (mustaḥillūn). The reason is that they disbelieve by deeming it permissible (istihlāl), so the threat is actually for their disbelief, and the crime is mentioned only as a cause and path to disbelief. He then applies this to the Qur'anic verse (4:93) about killing a believer intentionally. The verse states eternal Hell as recompense. The author argues that this applies to one who kills a believer intentionally because the victim is a believer, i.e., killing him for his faith, which constitutes disbelief. He contrasts this with cases where intentional killing is mentioned without istihlāl; in those cases the law of retaliation applies, and the killer retains the name of faith and brotherhood, with hope for mercy. The verse is thus evidence that it refers to the mustaḥill. He then refutes the objection that the verse threatens killing, not disbelief, by pointing out that Allah matched the punishment with killing the mustaḥill, proving it is a threat for disbelief. He emphasizes that eternal punishment for a believer is false (bāṭil), so the verse cannot be about a believer who kills without istihlāl. Then he turns to verses without eternity: it is possible that they apply to believers, because punishing a believer according to his sin is not rationally impossible, nor is there any legal proof preventing it. He supports this with reports about angels and prophets seeking forgiveness for believers, and the command for believers to seek forgiveness for each other. He argues that seeking forgiveness for one who does not deserve punishment would be asking for injustice, which is disbelief. Therefore, forgiveness and pardon apply to those who deserve punishment but are forgiven by Allah's bounty. He concludes by affirming Allah's names like Ghāfir, Ghafūr, Ghaffār, and ʿAfūw, and that these cannot be empty. By consensus, these apply to Muslims who sin without istihlāl, proving that sin does not entail disbelief or removal from faith, and that forgiveness for sins less than kufr is possible.

Key Terms
الأصل
الوعيد
الخلود
المستحل
الكفر
الاستحلال
الإيمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 4:93 means that any believer who murders will be eternally punished in Hell.
Correction: The author argues that the verse only applies to those who deem the killing permissible (istihlal), which constitutes disbelief, not to a believer who kills without istihlal.

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidi principle, what do verses of eternal threat refer to?

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi principle restricts eternal threat verses to those who deem the sin permissible, not to believing sinners.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself making excuses for a sin in your heart, treating it as 'no big deal' or 'permissible for me in my situation'? What does that say about your iman, and how does this lesson's warning against istihlal call you to repentance?
?
When you read a severe threat verse like Q. 4:93, do you immediately think it applies to 'other' sinful people, or do you honestly examine your own soul for hidden istihlal? Which response is more spiritually dangerous?
?
If you have committed a major sin, do you secretly believe you are beyond hope of mercy? How does this lesson's distinction between the mustahill and the believing sinner challenge that despair, while also demanding you uphold the sanctity of Allah's prohibitions?

Non-Eternal Threats and the Possibility of Forgiveness
Sanusi discusses non-eternal threats and the permissibility of forgiving sinful believers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why verses of threat without mention of eternity can apply to believers, and how istighfar, divine forgiveness, and divine names support the possibility of forgiveness for major sins without disbelief.

Core Idea
Threats without eternity allow for divine forgiveness; sin does not expel from faith.

Having established that eternal threats target mustaḥillūn, Sanusi now turns to verses that do not mention eternity. He argues that these can apply to believers, because punishing a believer for his sin is not rationally or scripturally impossible. He supports this with evidence from the Quran and Sunnah: angels and prophets seek forgiveness for believers, believers are commanded to seek forgiveness for each other, and Allah’s names like al-Ghafūr and al-ʿAfūw indicate that forgiveness of sins less than kufr is actual. This lesson shows how the Maturidi position preserves the possibility of forgiveness for sinful believers.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening of the second section
Start from 'وأما ما ليس بمقرون بذكر الخلود'. This is the contrast: verses without eternity can apply to believers.
Notice the phrase 'فمن الجائز أن يكون فى المؤمن'.
2
Examine the rational and scriptural basis
Sanusi says punishing a believer according to his sin is not rationally impossible (qabīḥ), nor is there a legal proof against it. He mentions reports (akhbār) supporting this.
He is refuting the Mu'tazilite claim that punishment of a believer is impossible.
3
Understand the istighfar argument
He argues that seeking forgiveness for someone who does not deserve punishment would be asking for injustice, which is disbelief. Therefore, those for whom istighfar is made must deserve punishment, i.e., believing sinners.
This implies that believers can sin and deserve punishment, but still be forgiven.
4
Connect to divine names
He mentions Allah's names Ghāfir, Ghafūr, Ghaffār, and ʿAfūw. These names imply that forgiveness is actualized for some. They cannot be empty. By consensus (ijmāʿ), they do not apply to disbelievers, so they must apply to sinful believers.
This is a powerful theological argument from the names of Allah.
5
Conclude the lesson
The final conclusion: the servant does not disbelieve by committing a sin (maʿṣiya) and does not leave faith by a major sin (kabīra). This allows for divine pardon and forgiveness.
This is the Maturidi position that sin does not negate faith.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأصل عندنا أن ما كان من الآيات الواردة في الوعيد مقرونا بذكر الخلود فهو من المستحلّين لذلك، لما أنهم كفروا لاستحلال ذلك فأوعدوا على كفرهم في الحقيقة وذكر تلك الجريمة لكونها سببًا للكفر وطريقاً إليه، ولهذا قلنا في تأويل قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَمَن يَقْتُلْ مُؤْمِنًا مُتَعَمِّدًا فَجَزَاؤُهُ جَهَنَّمُ خَلِدًا فِيهَا ﴾ أي متعمدًا الإماتة أي قتله لأجل أنه مؤمن ومن هذا قصده في القتل يكون كافرًا ألا ترى أنه حيث ذكر قتل العمد لا على الاستحلال فمن باب إيجاب القصاص بقي اسم الإيمان والأخوة بينه وبين المؤمنين وجعله أملاً للرحمة والتخفيف على ما بينا، فكان ذلك دليلاً أن هذه الآية وردت في المستحل وما يزعمون أن هذا وعيد القتل لا وعيد الكفر لأنه تعالى قابله بقتل المؤمن دون الكافر، والاستحلال كفر. قلنا هذا وعيد الكفر والله قابله بقتل المستحل لما أنه به يكفر فيكون جعل جزاء الكفر بمقابلته دليلاً على أنه كفر وإعلاما به على أنه لا وجه إلى جعله جزاء للقتل بدون الكفر لما أنه مؤمن وثبت ذلك بالدليل، والقول بخلود المؤمن في النار باطل فلا يمكنهم القول بأن هذا جزاء قتله إلا بعد إثبات زوال إيمانه وجعله في منزلة بين منزلتين أو القول بكفره والقول به باطل على ما قررنا، فدل أنه ليس بجزاء للقتل المطلق بل هو جزاء للقتل بطريق الاستحلال الذي هو كفر، وأما ما ليس بمقرون بذكر الخلود فمن الجائز أن يكون فى المؤمن إذ تعذيب المؤمن بقدر ذنبه ليس بمستحيل في العقل ولا قام دليل شرعي على امتناعه، ووردت به أخبار كثيرة ثم ثبت استغفار الملائكة والأنبياء عليهم السلام للمؤمنين، وكذا المؤمنون أمروا بالاستغفار بعضهم لبعض والاستغفار لمن لا يجز تعذيبه سؤال ترك الظلم وهذا كفر، وكذا مغفرة من لم يستوجب العذاب محال وكذا العفو دل أن من العباد من يستوجب العقوبة ثم الله تعالى يغفر له بفضله ويعفو عنه. يحققه أنه تعالى غافر الذنب، وكذا يسمى غفورًا وغفارا، ورد بهذه الأسامي نصوص الكتاب، وكذا الله تعالى عفو وتسمية الله تعالى بما لا يتحقق منه ومدحه نفسه بما لا يتصور منه محال، ثم بالإجماع ليس ذلك في حق المكذبين ولا الخارجين عن الإسلام، فدل أن ذلك في حق من ارتكب المعصية من غير الاستحلال فكان في دليل أن العبد بالمعصية لا يكفر وبالكبيرة لا يخرج عن الإيمان ليتحقق عفوه والمغفرة له ودليل جواز مغفرة ما دون الكفر إذ لولا ذلك لما تحقق
And the principle with us is that whatever verses have come down in threat conjoined with the mention of eternity (khulūd), they are [interpreted as referring to] those who deem [the sin] permissible (mustaḥillūn), because they disbelieved due to their deeming it permissible, so they were threatened on account of their disbelief in reality, and that crime is mentioned because it is a cause for disbelief and a path to it. For this reason we said in the interpretation of His statement, the Exalted: 'And whoever kills a believer intentionally, his recompense is Hell, abiding therein forever' – meaning intentionally doing away [with life], i.e., killing him because he is a believer, and whoever has this intention in killing becomes a disbeliever. Do you not see that when He mentioned intentional killing not on [the basis of] deeming permissible, it falls under the obligation of retaliation (qiṣāṣ), the name of faith and brotherhood remained between him and the believers, and He made him hopeful for mercy and leniency as we have explained? So that was evidence that this verse was revealed concerning the one who deems [it] permissible. And what they claim that this is a threat for killing, not a threat for disbelief, because He, Exalted, matched it with killing a believer, not a disbeliever – and deeming permissible is disbelief. We said: This is a threat for disbelief, and Allah matched it with killing the one who deems permissible because he disbelieves thereby, so making the recompense of disbelief correspond to it is evidence that it is disbelief and an announcement of it, and that there is no justification for making it a recompense for killing without disbelief because he is a believer, and that is established by evidence. The statement that a believer abides eternally in the Fire is false, so they cannot say that this is the recompense for his killing except after establishing the removal of his faith and placing him in a station between two stations, or saying that he is a disbeliever – and saying that is false according to what we have established. So it indicates that it is not a recompense for absolute killing, but rather a recompense for killing by way of deeming permissible, which is disbelief. As for what is not conjoined with the mention of eternity, it is possible that it applies to the believer, because punishing the believer according to his sin is not impossible in reason, nor has any legal proof (dalīl sharʿī) been established to prevent it, and many reports (akhbār) have come about it. Then it is established that the angels and the prophets, peace be upon them, seek forgiveness (istighfār) for the believers, and likewise the believers are commanded to seek forgiveness for one another. Seeking forgiveness for one who does not deserve punishment is asking for the abandonment of injustice, and this is disbelief. Likewise, forgiveness (maghfira) for one who does not deserve punishment is impossible, and likewise pardon (ʿafw) indicates that among the servants are those who deserve punishment, then Allah the Exalted forgives him by His bounty and pardons him. Confirming this: He, Exalted, is the Forgiver of sin (ghāfir al-dhanb), and likewise He is named Ghafūr and Ghaffār; the texts of the Book have come with these names. And likewise Allah the Exalted is ʿAfūw. Naming Allah with what is not actualized in Him, and His praising Himself with what is not conceivable from Him, is impossible. Then by consensus (ijmāʿ), that does not apply to the deniers (mukadhdhibīn) nor to those who have left Islam. So it indicates that that applies to the one who committed disobedience without deeming it permissible. Thus it was an evidence that the servant does not disbelieve by disobedience and does not leave faith by a major sin, so that His pardon and forgiveness may be actualized for him. And evidence for the permissibility of forgiving what is less than disbelief, for if not for that, it would not be actualized.
Source pages p. 394

Commentary
This passage expounds the Maturidi position on interpreting verses of threat (āyāt al-waʿīd) that include the mention of eternity (khulūd). The author begins by stating the fundamental principle (al-aṣl ʿindanā): any threat verse that is conjoined with the mention of eternity refers specifically to those who deem the sin permissible (mustaḥillūn). The reason is that they disbelieve by deeming it permissible (istihlāl), so the threat is actually for their disbelief, and the crime is mentioned only as a cause and path to disbelief. He then applies this to the Qur'anic verse (4:93) about killing a believer intentionally. The verse states eternal Hell as recompense. The author argues that this applies to one who kills a believer intentionally because the victim is a believer, i.e., killing him for his faith, which constitutes disbelief. He contrasts this with cases where intentional killing is mentioned without istihlāl; in those cases the law of retaliation applies, and the killer retains the name of faith and brotherhood, with hope for mercy. The verse is thus evidence that it refers to the mustaḥill. He then refutes the objection that the verse threatens killing, not disbelief, by pointing out that Allah matched the punishment with killing the mustaḥill, proving it is a threat for disbelief. He emphasizes that eternal punishment for a believer is false (bāṭil), so the verse cannot be about a believer who kills without istihlāl. Then he turns to verses without eternity: it is possible that they apply to believers, because punishing a believer according to his sin is not rationally impossible, nor is there any legal proof preventing it. He supports this with reports about angels and prophets seeking forgiveness for believers, and the command for believers to seek forgiveness for each other. He argues that seeking forgiveness for one who does not deserve punishment would be asking for injustice, which is disbelief. Therefore, forgiveness and pardon apply to those who deserve punishment but are forgiven by Allah's bounty. He concludes by affirming Allah's names like Ghāfir, Ghafūr, Ghaffār, and ʿAfūw, and that these cannot be empty. By consensus, these apply to Muslims who sin without istihlāl, proving that sin does not entail disbelief or removal from faith, and that forgiveness for sins less than kufr is possible.

When you ask others to pray for your forgiveness, do you do so with the awareness that you stand guilty before Allah, yet hopeful in His bounty? How can this lesson change the way you approach the prayer 'O Allah, forgive me'?

Refutation of the Claim That Killing a Believer Entails Disbelief
The author refutes the claim that the threat of eternal punishment for killing a believer proves the killer becomes a disbeliever.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Qur'anic threat of eternal punishment for killing a believer does not make the killer a disbeliever, but rather applies only when the killing is deemed permissible (istihlal).

Core Idea
Eternal punishment for killing a believer is tied to deeming it permissible, not to the act alone.

In this passage, the author addresses a common objection based on a Qur'anic verse that threatens eternal punishment for killing a believer. Some argued this proves the killer becomes a disbeliever. The author carefully analyzes the verse to show the threat is actually for those who deem murder permissible, an act of disbelief itself, not for the killing per se. This lesson unpacks that refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Identify the objection being refuted: that the Qur'anic threat of eternal punishment for killing a believer proves the killer becomes a kafir.
2
Identify the author's key move
Notice that the author links the threat to 'istihlal' (deeming permissible). He argues that the Qur'an threatened disbelief, not absolute killing. Deeming killing permissible is itself an act of disbelief.
3
Understand the implications
If the threat were for killing alone, it would require either the Mu'tazili position of 'al-manzila bayn al-manzilatayn' (a station between two stations) or declaring the killer a disbeliever outright. Both are false, so the threat cannot be for mere killing.
4
Conclude the lesson
The author concludes that the verse actually supports the view that the punishment is for istihlal, not for killing a believer per se. This refutes the objection that killing a believer entails disbelief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعيد الكفر لأنه تعالى قابله بقتل المؤمن دون الكافر، والاستحلال كفر. قلنا هذا وعيد الكفر والله قابله بقتل المستحل لما أنه به يكفر فيكون جعل جزاء الكفر بمقابلته دليلاً على أنه كفر وإعلاما به على أنه لا وجه إلى جعله جزاء للقتل بدون الكفر لما أنه مؤمن وثبت ذلك بالدليل، والقول بخلود المؤمن في النار باطل فلا يمكنهم القول بأن هذا جزاء قتله إلا بعد إثبات زوال إيمانه وجعله في منزلة بين منزلتين أو القول بكفره والقول به باطل على ما قررنا، فدل أنه ليس بجزاء للقتل المطلق بل هو جزاء للقتل بطريق الاستحلال الذي هو كفر
The threat of disbelief because He (Allah) mentioned it alongside the killing of a believer, not the disbeliever, and deeming (it) permissible is disbelief. We said: This is a threat of disbelief, and Allah mentioned it alongside the killing of the one who deems (it) permissible because he thereby disbelieves. So making the recompense of disbelief in correspondence with it is evidence that it is disbelief and an announcement of it, that there is no way to make it a recompense for killing without disbelief since he is a believer, and that is established by evidence. And the statement that a believer abides eternally in the Fire is false, so they cannot say that this is a recompense for his killing except after establishing the removal of his faith and placing him in a station between the two stations, or declaring him a disbeliever, and that statement is false according to what we have established. So it indicates that it is not a recompense for absolute killing, but rather a recompense for killing by way of deeming (it) permissible, which is disbelief.
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection. The objectors claim that the Qur'anic threat of eternal punishment for killing a believer proves that the killer becomes a disbeliever. The author responds by analyzing the verse. He points out that the threat is specifically linked to the killing of a believer, not a disbeliever, and that deeming murder permissible (istihlal) is itself an act of disbelief. Thus, the threat is actually a threat of disbelief, not a recompense for the mere act of killing. The author argues that if the punishment were for the killing alone, it would imply that a believer who kills another believer becomes an unbeliever and deserves eternal hell. But that contradicts the fact that a believer can be punished temporarily. So the only way for the objectors to maintain their claim is to adopt the Mu'tazila position of a station between two stations (al-manzila bayn al-manzilatayn) or to declare the killer a disbeliever outright, both of which are false according to earlier established proofs. Therefore, the verse actually shows that the recompense is for killing accompanied by istihlal, which is disbelief, not for the killing per se.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that the Qur'an's threat of eternal punishment for killing a believer means that every believer who kills another believer becomes a disbeliever and will remain in Hell forever.
Correction: The threat is not for killing per se but for deeming it permissible (istihlal), which is an act of disbelief. A believer who kills without deeming it permissible may be punished temporarily, not eternally.

Checkpoint

B) That the threat is only for killing accompanied by deeming it permissible (istihlal) which is an act of disbelief
C) That the killer will be punished eternally regardless of his faith
D) That the punishment is a recompense for the act of killing a believer

Key Takeaway
The Qur'anic threat of eternal punishment for killing a believer is recompense for the disbelief of deeming it permissible, not for the killing itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you see another Muslim commit a major sin, do you immediately question their faith? How does this passage call you to withhold judgment and instead focus on correcting the action without declaring disbelief?

Al-Maturidi as Waqifi: Misunderstanding of His Position
Defining al-Maturidi as waqifi in action, not belief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that al-Maturidi was called 'waqifi' out of ignorance, and that his actual position was to act on general texts while suspending judgment in belief regarding the ultimate fate of grave sinners.

Core Idea
Al-Maturidi was waqifi in practice, not in belief—a crucial distinction.

The great Maturidi theologian Abu Mansur al-Maturidi was sometimes labeled 'waqifi' (one who suspends judgment) regarding the threat of punishment for grave sinners. In this passage, the author defends al-Maturidi, explaining that his followers misunderstood him. The key is to distinguish between suspending judgment in action (which al-Maturidi did) and suspending belief (which he did not). This lesson clarifies that distinction and sets the stage for understanding his methodological principles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the accusation
Notice that al-Maturidi is called 'waqifi' by his followers. The author says this is out of ignorance.
Pay attention to the phrase 'جهلاً منهم بمذهبه'.
2
Understand the definition of waqifi
The author defines waqifi as one who suspends judgment both in action and belief. Consider what this implies.
3
Distinguish between action and belief
Al-Maturidi's actual position is that acting on general texts is wajib, but he suspends final judgment about the fate of sinners. This is waqif in action, not belief.
This is the core idea.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أطلق في كتابه أن الفاسق المطلق هو صاحب الكبيرة ولست أدري أقال ذلك علمًا منه بما يؤدي إليه هذا القول أم سنح له شيء فتكلم به من غير العلم بما يؤدي إليه والسرائر موكولة إلى الله تعالى وهو أعلم بالعقائد والضمائر، فاتبعه جميع المنتسبين إلى التحقيق في ذلك واقتدوا به وما اشتغل أحد منهم أنه بأي طريق يتكلم في مسألة الوعيد، وسموا الشيخ أبا منصور رحمه الله الواقفي في هذه المسألة جهلاً منهم بمذهبه إذ حقيقة مذهبه أن العمل بالعموم وبمطلق الصيغة واجب، إذ الواجب هو ما ثبت بدليل غير مقطوع به وهو دليل الظاهر، فأما ما ثبت بدليل مقطوع به فهو الفرض، ولم يرد عن أحد من متقدمي أصحابنا أنهم قالوا مطلق الأمر للوجوب والقول بالعموم واجب وهو ما ذهب إليه الشيخ أبو منصور، الواقفي فهو من يقف في ذلك عملا واعتقادًا، ثم إن من ساعد هذا القائل بالعموم وظهر منه القول بجواز مغفرة صاحب الكبيرة يضيق عليه الأمر بطريق التخصيص كما ذهب إليه الشيخ أبو منصور، وجميع من ساعدنا في هذه المسألة من فحول المتكلمين كأبي الحسن الأشعري و من تابعه وابن الراوندي والحسين وغيرهم
He stated in his book that the absolute sinner is the one who commits a major sin, and I do not know whether he said that knowingly of what this statement leads to, or something occurred to him and he spoke without knowledge of what it leads to—secrets are entrusted to God Almighty, and He knows best beliefs and consciences. So all those who claim to be investigators followed him in that and imitated him, and none of them concerned themselves with by what method he speaks on the issue of the threat. They called Shaykh Abu Mansur (God have mercy on him) 'al-waqifi' in this issue, out of ignorance of his doctrine. For the reality of his doctrine is that acting upon the general and the absolute form of the wording is obligatory; indeed, the obligatory (al-wajib) is that which is established by a non-definitive proof, which is the apparent proof. As for that which is established by a definitive proof, it is the required (al-fard). It has not been reported from any of our early companions that they said the absolute command indicates obligation, and the statement of generality is obligatory—that is what Shaykh Abu Mansur held. 'Al-waqifi' is one who stops [suspends judgment] in that both in practice and belief. Then, indeed, whoever supports this proponent of generality and from whom appears the statement of the permissibility of forgiveness for the grave sinner—his matter becomes constrained by way of specification (al-takhsis), just as Shaykh Abu Mansur held, and all who supported us in this issue from the leading theologians, such as Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and those who followed him, and Ibn al-Rawandi, and al-Husayn, and others.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage defines the position of Abu Mansur al-Maturidi regarding the interpretation of general threats against grave sinners. The author begins by noting that al-Maturidi called a 'fasiq mutlaq' one who commits a major sin, but the author expresses uncertainty whether al-Maturidi knew the theological consequences of that statement. He then explains that followers of al-Maturidi misunderstood him by calling him a 'waqifi' (one who suspends judgment) on the issue of the threat, when in reality al-Maturidi held that acting upon general texts (al-umum) is obligatory (wajib), which is based on non-definitive evidence (dalil al-zahir). The author distinguishes between 'wajib' (established by non-definitive proof) and 'fard' (established by definitive proof), noting that early Maturidi scholars did not claim that the absolute command indicates obligation. Al-Maturidi's actual position, according to the author, is that one who speaks of umum but allows forgiveness to the grave sinner must narrow the application through specification (takhsis). The author lists other theologians who agreed: Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, his followers, Ibn al-Rawandi, al-Husayn, and others, indicating a broad consensus on this methodological approach.

العموم
الواجب
الفرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That al-Maturidi was a waqifi in belief, i.e., he doubted the reality of divine punishment for grave sinners.
Correction: In fact, al-Maturidi only suspended judgment on the specific application of punishment to individual sinners, while affirming the general threat as a true statement from God.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did al-Maturidi's followers call him 'waqifi'?
A) Because he suspended judgment in belief
B) Because he suspended judgment in both action and belief
C) Out of ignorance of his doctrine
D) Because he denied the threat entirely

Key Takeaway
Al-Maturidi was called waqifi out of ignorance; in reality, he affirmed acting on general texts and only suspended final judgment in action, not belief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The author says 'secrets are entrusted to God' when speaking of al-Maturidi's intention. Do you extend that same charity to those you disagree with, or do you assume you know their hearts?
?
When you learn that a term like 'waqifi' can be misunderstood, does it make you more cautious about how you judge labels in your own life? What label do you need to re-examine today?

The Principle of Takhsis in the Interpretation of General Threats
Explaining the use of takhsis to resolve the tension between general threats and forgiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Maturidi and other theologians used the principle of specification (takhsis) to reconcile the general threat against grave sinners with the possibility of forgiveness.

Core Idea
General threats are real, but they can be specified to allow for forgiveness.

Having established that acting on general texts is wajib, al-Maturidi faced the challenge of how to understand the Quranic verses and hadith that threaten grave sinners with punishment. If these texts are general, do they apply to every sinner without exception? In this passage, the author explains that al-Maturidi and many other leading theologians—including Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari—used the principle of specification (takhsis) to narrow the application of these threats. This lesson explores that methodological move.

Guided Path
1
Identify the methodological problem
The author says that whoever speaks of umum (generality) must also allow room for specification. Understand why this is necessary.
The problem: if a general threat includes all sinners, how can anyone be forgiven?
2
Recognize the use of takhsis
Al-Maturidi and many others used takhsis to reconcile the general threat with the possibility of forgiveness. This is a standard Kalam method.
Takhsis is the narrowing of a general ruling.
3
Note the consensus among theologians
The author lists al-Ash'ari, Ibn al-Rawandi, al-Husayn, and others as agreeing with this approach.
This shows a broad Sunni consensus, not just Maturidi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أطلق في كتابه أن الفاسق المطلق هو صاحب الكبيرة ولست أدري أقال ذلك علمًا منه بما يؤدي إليه هذا القول أم سنح له شيء فتكلم به من غير العلم بما يؤدي إليه والسرائر موكولة إلى الله تعالى وهو أعلم بالعقائد والضمائر، فاتبعه جميع المنتسبين إلى التحقيق في ذلك واقتدوا به وما اشتغل أحد منهم أنه بأي طريق يتكلم في مسألة الوعيد، وسموا الشيخ أبا منصور رحمه الله الواقفي في هذه المسألة جهلاً منهم بمذهبه إذ حقيقة مذهبه أن العمل بالعموم وبمطلق الصيغة واجب، إذ الواجب هو ما ثبت بدليل غير مقطوع به وهو دليل الظاهر، فأما ما ثبت بدليل مقطوع به فهو الفرض، ولم يرد عن أحد من متقدمي أصحابنا أنهم قالوا مطلق الأمر للوجوب والقول بالعموم واجب وهو ما ذهب إليه الشيخ أبو منصور، الواقفي فهو من يقف في ذلك عملا واعتقادًا، ثم إن من ساعد هذا القائل بالعموم وظهر منه القول بجواز مغفرة صاحب الكبيرة يضيق عليه الأمر بطريق التخصيص كما ذهب إليه الشيخ أبو منصور، وجميع من ساعدنا في هذه المسألة من فحول المتكلمين كأبي الحسن الأشعري و من تابعه وابن الراوندي والحسين وغيرهم
He stated in his book that the absolute sinner is the one who commits a major sin, and I do not know whether he said that knowingly of what this statement leads to, or something occurred to him and he spoke without knowledge of what it leads to—secrets are entrusted to God Almighty, and He knows best beliefs and consciences. So all those who claim to be investigators followed him in that and imitated him, and none of them concerned themselves with by what method he speaks on the issue of the threat. They called Shaykh Abu Mansur (God have mercy on him) 'al-waqifi' in this issue, out of ignorance of his doctrine. For the reality of his doctrine is that acting upon the general and the absolute form of the wording is obligatory; indeed, the obligatory (al-wajib) is that which is established by a non-definitive proof, which is the apparent proof. As for that which is established by a definitive proof, it is the required (al-fard). It has not been reported from any of our early companions that they said the absolute command indicates obligation, and the statement of generality is obligatory—that is what Shaykh Abu Mansur held. 'Al-waqifi' is one who stops [suspends judgment] in that both in practice and belief. Then, indeed, whoever supports this proponent of generality and from whom appears the statement of the permissibility of forgiveness for the grave sinner—his matter becomes constrained by way of specification (al-takhsis), just as Shaykh Abu Mansur held, and all who supported us in this issue from the leading theologians, such as Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and those who followed him, and Ibn al-Rawandi, and al-Husayn, and others.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage defines the position of Abu Mansur al-Maturidi regarding the interpretation of general threats against grave sinners. The author begins by noting that al-Maturidi called a 'fasiq mutlaq' one who commits a major sin, but the author expresses uncertainty whether al-Maturidi knew the theological consequences of that statement. He then explains that followers of al-Maturidi misunderstood him by calling him a 'waqifi' (one who suspends judgment) on the issue of the threat, when in reality al-Maturidi held that acting upon general texts (al-umum) is obligatory (wajib), which is based on non-definitive evidence (dalil al-zahir). The author distinguishes between 'wajib' (established by non-definitive proof) and 'fard' (established by definitive proof), noting that early Maturidi scholars did not claim that the absolute command indicates obligation. Al-Maturidi's actual position, according to the author, is that one who speaks of umum but allows forgiveness to the grave sinner must narrow the application through specification (takhsis). The author lists other theologians who agreed: Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, his followers, Ibn al-Rawandi, al-Husayn, and others, indicating a broad consensus on this methodological approach.

Key Terms
التخصيص
العموم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That general threats cannot be limited; they apply to all sinners equally without exception.
Correction: Using specification (takhsis), general threats can be applied only to specific categories of sinners, allowing for forgiveness for others.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what method did al-Maturidi use to reconcile general threats with forgiveness?
A) Abrogation (naskh)
B) Specification (takhsis)
C) Rejection (rafd)
D) Postponement (irja')

Key Takeaway
Al-Maturidi and other Sunni theologians used specification (takhsis) to allow forgiveness for grave sinners while affirming the general threat.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does the principle of takhsis challenge your tendency to either despair of God's mercy or take it for granted? In light of this teaching, what specific step can you take this week to balance hope and fear in your relationship with Allah?
?
The scholars affirmed that God's general threat of punishment is real but can be specified to allow forgiveness. Are there sins in your life for which you assume automatic forgiveness without repentance? What would it mean for you to take the threat seriously while trusting in God's mercy?
?
This passage shows that leading theologians from different schools agreed on this methodology. How does being part of that scholarly tradition affect your responsibility to learn and apply Islamic principles to your own life? What is one teaching from this lesson you will actively try to embody starting today?

The Dilemma of the Major Sinner Between Promise and Threat
Dialectical dilemma showing contradiction in unqualified general verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why insisting on unqualified generality in verses of promise and threat leads to an unavoidable contradiction.

Core Idea
Unqualified general verses force a choice between kufr and self-contradiction.

The author begins by asking the opponent to admit that both verses of promise and threat are general. He then considers a major sinner who has faith and does good deeds. This person seems included in the promise, but also subject to the threat. The opponent cannot place him without either accusing Allah of lying or abandoning his school. This lesson walks through that dilemma.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening question
The author establishes that the opponent accepts general verses of promise and threat. Note the key example verse: 18:107.
2
Understand the case of the major sinner
Identify the sinner who has faith but commits major sins like consuming orphans' wealth or murder. He is included in the verse of promise.
3
Examine the two possible answers
The opponent must say either he enters Paradise or Hell. Each leads to a problem: if Paradise, it contradicts the threat; if Hell, it contradicts the promise.
4
See the extension to apostasy and repentance
The author adds cases of apostasy and repentance, showing the same dilemma applies.
5
Recognize the forced choice
The author forces the opponent into a choice: either insist on unqualified generality and call Allah a liar (kufr), or admit conditions and contradict his own school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل لهم: أليس أن بعض آيات الوعد وردت عامة، كما أن بعض آيات الوعيد وردت عامة؟ فلا بد من بلى لوجود ذلك في نحو قوله تعالى: ﴿ إِنَّ الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا وَعَمِلُوا الصَّالِحَاتِ كَانَتْ لَهُمْ جَنَّاتُ الْفِرْدَوْسِ نُزُلًا ﴾ وغير ذلك من الآيات. قيل: فإذا كان صاحب الكبيرة قد وجد منه الإيمان والأعمال الصالحة فهو من الذين آمنوا وعملوا الصالحات فصار كأنه ذكر باسمه الخاص وهو ممن قد أكل أموال اليتامى ظلمًا وكذا من قتل مسلما عمدًا ما حكمه؟ ومن أي قبيل هو؟ أكان ممن يكون في جهنم خالدا مخلدا؟ أو ممن كانت له جَنَّاتُ الْفِرْدَوْسِ نُزُلًا خَالِدِينَ فِيهَا لا يَبْغُونَ عَنْهَا حِوَلًا؟ فإن قال: هو ممن يدخل في الجنة فقد ترك مذهبه ووصف الله تعالى بالكذب حيث أخبر أنه يخلده في النار ولم يخلده. وإن قال: هو ممن يخلد في النار وهو مذهبه، قيل: أليس أنه كان داخلا في قوله تعالى: كَانَتْ لَهُمْ جَنَّاتُ الْفِرْدَوْسِ نُزُلًا خَالِدِينَ فِيهَا؟ فإن قال: لا فقد ترك مذهبه في تناول صيغة العموم كل فرد من أفراد العموم بطريق التعيين، وإن قال: نعم فقد زعم أن الله تعالى كذب في الإخبار عنه أنه يخلده في الجنة.

ثم نقول: أليس أن من آمن وعمل الصالحات ثم ارتد عن الإسلام نعوذ بالله يخلد في النار؟ فلا بد من بلى. قيل: وكذا كل من ارتكب كبيرة ثم تاب أو كان كافرا ثم أسلم ومات على إسلامه يخلد في الجنة ولا يخلد في النار؟ فلا بد من بلى. قيل: أليس أن الصيغة مطلقة في الوعد والوعيد جميعا لم يوجد في صيغة الوعد شرط الموت على الإيمان، ولا في صيغة الوعيد شرط الموت على الكفر والإصرار على المعصية؟ فلا بد من بلى. قيل: فأنت بين أمرين: إما أن تقول بأن الشرط غير ملحق بآيات الوعيد ولا بآيات الوعد وتناولت كل صيغة كل فرد من أفرادها وصار الله تعالى كاذبا بإدخال صاحب الكبيرة الذي تاب أو الكافر الذي أسلم الجنة وبإدخال المؤمن الذي ارتد وارتكب كبيرة النار، وإما أن تقول بأن قيد الموت على الإيمان أو الموت قبل التوبة ملتحق بصيغة الوعد والوعيد وإن لم يكن مقرونا به؟ فإن قلت بالأول فقد كفرت، وإن قلت بالثاني فقد تركت مذهبك وناقضت.

ثم كل جواب لك في إخراج من دخل تحت صيغة العموم الواردة في الوعد عن حكمها فهو الجواب لك في إخراج من دخل تحت صيغة الوعد عن حكمها. ولو قلت وردت آيات الوعد وآيات الوعيد وجهل تاريخ نزولها جعلت كأنها نزلت مقترنة فيصير البعض قيدا وتخصيصا في البعض، قيل: فلم كنت بجعلك آيات الوعيد قيدا في آيات الوعد أولى من خصمك بجعله آيات الوعد قيدا في آيات الوعيد؟ مع أنك إن جهلت التاريخ فجعلت كأنها وردت جملة حكما، فلا شك أنها نزلت متفرقة فلم يكن بد من نسبة الكذب إلى الله تعالى لا محالة، حيث لم يحقق الوعيد؟
It was said to them: Is it not that some verses of promise came generally, just as some verses of threat came generally? There is no avoiding 'yes' due to the existence of that in the likes of His statement, the Exalted: 'Indeed, those who have believed and done righteous deeds - they will have the Gardens of Paradise as a lodging' (18:107) and other such verses. It was said: So, if the committer of a major sin has from him faith and righteous deeds, he is among those who believed and did righteous deeds, thus he becomes as if mentioned by his specific name, while he is among those who consumed the wealth of orphans unjustly, and likewise the one who killed a Muslim intentionally - what is his ruling? And of which category is he? Is he among those who will be in Hell, eternally abiding? Or among those for whom are the Gardens of Paradise as a lodging, abiding eternally therein, not desiring any transfer? If he says: He is among those who enter Paradise, then he has abandoned his school and described Allah the Exalted as lying, since He informed that He will make him abide eternally in the Fire and did not make him abide. And if he says: He is among those who abide eternally in the Fire - and that is his school - it is said: Is he not included in the statement of the Exalted: 'They will have the Gardens of Paradise as a lodging, abiding eternally therein'? If he says: No, then he has abandoned his school regarding the general form's encompassing every individual of the general individuals by way of specification. And if he says: Yes, then he has claimed that Allah the Exalted lied in the informing about him that He will make him abide eternally in Paradise.

Then we say: Is it not that whoever believed and did righteous deeds then apostatized from Islam - we seek refuge with Allah - will abide eternally in the Fire? There is no avoiding 'yes'. It was said: And likewise, everyone who committed a major sin then repented, or was a disbeliever then became Muslim and died upon his Islam, will abide eternally in Paradise and will not abide eternally in the Fire? There is no avoiding 'yes'. It was said: Is it not that the form is unqualified in both promise and threat; there does not exist in the form of promise the condition of dying upon faith, nor in the form of threat the condition of dying upon disbelief and persistence in disobedience? There is no avoiding 'yes'. It was said: Then you are between two matters: Either you say that the condition is not attached to the verses of threat nor to the verses of promise, and every form encompasses every individual of its individuals, and Allah the Exalted becomes a liar by admitting the committer of a major sin who repented or the disbeliever who became Muslim into Paradise, and by admitting the believer who apostatized and committed a major sin into the Fire; or you say that the restriction of dying upon faith or dying before repentance is attached to the form of promise and threat even if not explicitly accompanied? If you say the first, then you have disbelieved; and if you say the second, then you have abandoned your school and contradicted yourself.

Then every answer of yours in excluding from its ruling one who falls under the general form occurring in threat is an answer for you in excluding from its ruling one who falls under the form of promise. And if you say: The verses of promise and verses of threat came and the history of their revelation is unknown - you make them as if they came together, so some become a restriction and specification for others - it is said: Why are you, by making the verses of threat a restriction upon the verses of promise, more entitled than your opponent who makes the verses of promise a restriction upon the verses of threat? Since if you are ignorant of the history, you make them as if they came as a whole as a ruling, but there is no doubt that they came separately, so there is no avoiding attributing lying to Allah the Exalted necessarily, since the threat was not fulfilled.
Source pages p. 407

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument (refutation) against an opponent who holds that general verses of promise (آيات الوعد) and general verses of threat (آيات الوعيد) apply without qualification to every individual mentioned. The author (Imam al-Nasafi) shows that this position leads to absurdity and either kufr or self-contradiction.

The argument proceeds in several steps:
1. The opponent admits that some verses of promise are general, just as some verses of threat are general. For example, 'Indeed, those who believe and do righteous deeds will have the Gardens of Paradise as a lodging' (18:107).
2. The author then considers a sinner who commits a major sin (e.g., consuming orphans' property unjustly or murder) yet also has faith and does some righteous deeds. According to the general verse of promise, such a person would be included among 'those who believe and do righteous deeds' and thus promised Paradise.
3. But the same person is also subject to verses of threat that threaten eternal punishment for such sins. The opponent is asked: is this person among those who enter Paradise or among those who abide eternally in Hell? If he says Paradise, he abandons his own school (which holds that major sinners abide eternally in Hell) and also accuses Allah of lying, since Allah has threatened eternal punishment for such a sinner. If he says Hell, he must explain how the general verse of promise does not include him. If he denies inclusion, he abandons his commitment to the unqualified generality of the form. If he affirms inclusion, he again accuses Allah of lying.
4. The argument then extends to cases of apostasy and repentance. A believer who apostatizes is threatened with eternal Fire. A sinner who repents or a disbeliever who becomes Muslim and dies in faith is promised eternal Paradise. The author notes that the Quranic forms are unqualified: no condition of dying upon faith is attached to promises, nor is dying upon disbelief attached to threats.
5. Thus the opponent faces a forced choice: either insist on unqualified generality (which makes Allah a liar, because the promise would apply to those who later apostatize and the threat to those who repent) or admit that conditions are implicitly attached (which contradicts his school's rejection of implicit conditioning). The first option is kufr; the second is self-contradiction.
6. Finally, the author notes that any attempt to exclude individuals from the general form of threat (by appealing to conditions) would equally apply to exclusions from the form of promise. If one claims ignorance of the order of revelation, making them seem simultaneous so that some verses restrict others, the opponent has no priority to make threat restrict promise rather than the reverse. Since the verses actually came separately, attributing lying to Allah cannot be avoided on any of these attempted escapes.

Key Terms
صيغة العموم
آيات الوعد
آيات الوعيد
صاحب الكبيرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That general verses of promise and threat apply absolutely without conditions.
Correction: This passage shows that unqualified generality leads to contradictions; therefore, conditions must be implicitly attached, such as dying upon faith for promise and dying upon disbelief for threat.

Checkpoint
According to this argument, what happens if the opponent insists on the unqualified generality of both verses of promise and verses of threat?
A) He can consistently maintain his position.
B) He is forced into either kufr or self-contradiction.
C) He must accept that all sinners go to heaven.
D) He must accept that all believers go to hell.

Key Takeaway
Unqualified generality in verses of promise and threat leads to an unavoidable dilemma that can only be resolved by admitting implicit conditions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses like 'Indeed, those who believe and do righteous deeds will have Gardens of Paradise' (18:107), do you automatically assume it applies to you no matter how you live? How does this lesson's dilemma force you to pause and examine the conditions you might be ignoring?
?
The opponent in this passage was faced with a choice between attributing falsehood to Allah and contradicting his own beliefs. Has there been a belief you held about Allah's mercy or punishment that you never fully thought through? What would it look like for you to honestly re-examine that belief today, even if it feels uncomfortable?
?
If you were to take one concrete step to ensure that your understanding of Allah's promise and threat is truly coherent and not based on assumptions, what would that step be? And when will you do it?

The Symmetry Argument and the Impossibility of Escape
Dialectical conclusion showing equal treatment and failure of historical ignorance escape.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the opponent's attempted escape by appealing to the order of revelation or by applying conditions inconsistently.

Core Idea
Any escape from the dilemma applies equally to both sides, and ignorance of revelation order does not help.

The author continues the argument by showing that the opponent cannot selectively exclude individuals from the threat verses without also excluding from promise verses. Moreover, claiming ignorance of which verses came first does not resolve the contradiction; it simply makes the outcome arbitrary. This lesson examines these final points.

Guided Path
1
Understand the symmetry argument
The author points out that any justification the opponent uses to exclude someone from the threat must also apply to exclude someone from the promise. This shows inconsistency.
2
Consider the opponent's possible escape
The opponent might say the order of revelation is unknown, so some verses might be abrogated or qualified by others.
3
See why the escape fails
The author asks why the opponent gives priority to threat verses over promise verses. Arbitrariness is no solution.
4
Recognize the conclusion
Since the verses were actually revealed separately, ignoring history makes Allah a liar, because the threat would be unfulfilled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

ثم نقول: أليس أن من آمن وعمل الصالحات ثم ارتد عن الإسلام نعوذ بالله يخلد في النار؟ فلا بد من بلى. قيل: وكذا كل من ارتكب كبيرة ثم تاب أو كان كافرا ثم أسلم ومات على إسلامه يخلد في الجنة ولا يخلد في النار؟ فلا بد من بلى. قيل: أليس أن الصيغة مطلقة في الوعد والوعيد جميعا لم يوجد في صيغة الوعد شرط الموت على الإيمان، ولا في صيغة الوعيد شرط الموت على الكفر والإصرار على المعصية؟ فلا بد من بلى. قيل: فأنت بين أمرين: إما أن تقول بأن الشرط غير ملحق بآيات الوعيد ولا بآيات الوعد وتناولت كل صيغة كل فرد من أفرادها وصار الله تعالى كاذبا بإدخال صاحب الكبيرة الذي تاب أو الكافر الذي أسلم الجنة وبإدخال المؤمن الذي ارتد وارتكب كبيرة النار، وإما أن تقول بأن قيد الموت على الإيمان أو الموت قبل التوبة ملتحق بصيغة الوعد والوعيد وإن لم يكن مقرونا به؟ فإن قلت بالأول فقد كفرت، وإن قلت بالثاني فقد تركت مذهبك وناقضت.

ثم كل جواب لك في إخراج من دخل تحت صيغة العموم الواردة في الوعد عن حكمها فهو الجواب لك في إخراج من دخل تحت صيغة الوعد عن حكمها. ولو قلت وردت آيات الوعد وآيات الوعيد وجهل تاريخ نزولها جعلت كأنها نزلت مقترنة فيصير البعض قيدا وتخصيصا في البعض، قيل: فلم كنت بجعلك آيات الوعيد قيدا في آيات الوعد أولى من خصمك بجعله آيات الوعد قيدا في آيات الوعيد؟ مع أنك إن جهلت التاريخ فجعلت كأنها وردت جملة حكما، فلا شك أنها نزلت متفرقة فلم يكن بد من نسبة الكذب إلى الله تعالى لا محالة، حيث لم يحقق الوعيد؟
It was said to them: Is it not that some verses of promise came generally, just as some verses of threat came generally? There is no avoiding 'yes' due to the existence of that in the likes of His statement, the Exalted: 'Indeed, those who have believed and done righteous deeds - they will have the Gardens of Paradise as a lodging' (18:107) and other such verses. It was said: So, if the committer of a major sin has from him faith and righteous deeds, he is among those who believed and did righteous deeds, thus he becomes as if mentioned by his specific name, while he is among those who consumed the wealth of orphans unjustly, and likewise the one who killed a Muslim intentionally - what is his ruling? And of which category is he? Is he among those who will be in Hell, eternally abiding? Or among those for whom are the Gardens of Paradise as a lodging, abiding eternally therein, not desiring any transfer? If he says: He is among those who enter Paradise, then he has abandoned his school and described Allah the Exalted as lying, since He informed that He will make him abide eternally in the Fire and did not make him abide. And if he says: He is among those who abide eternally in the Fire - and that is his school - it is said: Is he not included in the statement of the Exalted: 'They will have the Gardens of Paradise as a lodging, abiding eternally therein'? If he says: No, then he has abandoned his school regarding the general form's encompassing every individual of the general individuals by way of specification. And if he says: Yes, then he has claimed that Allah the Exalted lied in the informing about him that He will make him abide eternally in Paradise.

Then we say: Is it not that whoever believed and did righteous deeds then apostatized from Islam - we seek refuge with Allah - will abide eternally in the Fire? There is no avoiding 'yes'. It was said: And likewise, everyone who committed a major sin then repented, or was a disbeliever then became Muslim and died upon his Islam, will abide eternally in Paradise and will not abide eternally in the Fire? There is no avoiding 'yes'. It was said: Is it not that the form is unqualified in both promise and threat; there does not exist in the form of promise the condition of dying upon faith, nor in the form of threat the condition of dying upon disbelief and persistence in disobedience? There is no avoiding 'yes'. It was said: Then you are between two matters: Either you say that the condition is not attached to the verses of threat nor to the verses of promise, and every form encompasses every individual of its individuals, and Allah the Exalted becomes a liar by admitting the committer of a major sin who repented or the disbeliever who became Muslim into Paradise, and by admitting the believer who apostatized and committed a major sin into the Fire; or you say that the restriction of dying upon faith or dying before repentance is attached to the form of promise and threat even if not explicitly accompanied? If you say the first, then you have disbelieved; and if you say the second, then you have abandoned your school and contradicted yourself.

Then every answer of yours in excluding from its ruling one who falls under the general form occurring in threat is an answer for you in excluding from its ruling one who falls under the form of promise. And if you say: The verses of promise and verses of threat came and the history of their revelation is unknown - you make them as if they came together, so some become a restriction and specification for others - it is said: Why are you, by making the verses of threat a restriction upon the verses of promise, more entitled than your opponent who makes the verses of promise a restriction upon the verses of threat? Since if you are ignorant of the history, you make them as if they came as a whole as a ruling, but there is no doubt that they came separately, so there is no avoiding attributing lying to Allah the Exalted necessarily, since the threat was not fulfilled.
Source pages p. 407

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument (refutation) against an opponent who holds that general verses of promise (آيات الوعد) and general verses of threat (آيات الوعيد) apply without qualification to every individual mentioned. The author (Imam al-Nasafi) shows that this position leads to absurdity and either kufr or self-contradiction.

The argument proceeds in several steps:
1. The opponent admits that some verses of promise are general, just as some verses of threat are general. For example, 'Indeed, those who believe and do righteous deeds will have the Gardens of Paradise as a lodging' (18:107).
2. The author then considers a sinner who commits a major sin (e.g., consuming orphans' property unjustly or murder) yet also has faith and does some righteous deeds. According to the general verse of promise, such a person would be included among 'those who believe and do righteous deeds' and thus promised Paradise.
3. But the same person is also subject to verses of threat that threaten eternal punishment for such sins. The opponent is asked: is this person among those who enter Paradise or among those who abide eternally in Hell? If he says Paradise, he abandons his own school (which holds that major sinners abide eternally in Hell) and also accuses Allah of lying, since Allah has threatened eternal punishment for such a sinner. If he says Hell, he must explain how the general verse of promise does not include him. If he denies inclusion, he abandons his commitment to the unqualified generality of the form. If he affirms inclusion, he again accuses Allah of lying.
4. The argument then extends to cases of apostasy and repentance. A believer who apostatizes is threatened with eternal Fire. A sinner who repents or a disbeliever who becomes Muslim and dies in faith is promised eternal Paradise. The author notes that the Quranic forms are unqualified: no condition of dying upon faith is attached to promises, nor is dying upon disbelief attached to threats.
5. Thus the opponent faces a forced choice: either insist on unqualified generality (which makes Allah a liar, because the promise would apply to those who later apostatize and the threat to those who repent) or admit that conditions are implicitly attached (which contradicts his school's rejection of implicit conditioning). The first option is kufr; the second is self-contradiction.
6. Finally, the author notes that any attempt to exclude individuals from the general form of threat (by appealing to conditions) would equally apply to exclusions from the form of promise. If one claims ignorance of the order of revelation, making them seem simultaneous so that some verses restrict others, the opponent has no priority to make threat restrict promise rather than the reverse. Since the verses actually came separately, attributing lying to Allah cannot be avoided on any of these attempted escapes.

Key Terms
التخصيص
النسخ
التاريخ
العموم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That claiming ignorance of the order of revelation resolves the contradiction between general verses of promise and threat.
Correction: Ignoring the order does not solve the problem; it simply makes the resolution arbitrary and still leads to accusing Allah of lying.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main argument against the opponent's attempt to escape by claiming ignorance of the order of revelation?
A) The order of revelation is known, so it is not a valid excuse.
B) Giving priority to threat verses over promise verses is arbitrary and both sides would lead to contradiction.
C) The verses of promise are always later, so they abrogate the threat.
D) Allah would never lie, so the threat must be fulfilled as stated.

Key Takeaway
Any attempt to escape the dilemma by claiming ignorance or applying inconsistent conditions fails; the only consistent position is that general verses are implicitly conditioned.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses of Allah's mercy, do you accept them with full confidence, but when you read verses of warning, do you look for ways to soften them or explain them away? What does this asymmetry reveal about your heart?
?
If you were to apply the same standard to yourself that you apply to others when it comes to Allah's promise and threat, would you be more afraid or more hopeful? Are you willing to be consistent?
?
The argument shows that ignoring the order of revelation to suit your preference leads to accusing Allah of lying. Where in your own understanding of the Qur'an do you subtly prioritize what comforts you over what challenges you, and what would it cost you to submit fully to both?

Proof of Intercession for Major Sinners
Nasafi gives a brief rational proof for intercession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological basis for the permissibility of intercession for major sinners in Sunni Islam, and how it follows logically from God's grace and wisdom.

Core Idea
If God can forgive directly, He can forgive through intercession.

Imam Nasafi presents a concise argument for the doctrine of intercession (shafa'a). He links it directly to the principle that forgiveness is entirely within God's prerogative by His grace and mercy. The logic is simple: if God may forgive a major sinner without any intermediary, He may also do so through the intercession of prophets and saints. This passage affirms the Sunni position against those who deny intercession, such as the Mu'tazila.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once and then the literal translation. Notice the compact style and the logical flow from the first issue to intercession.
2
Identify key terms
Look at the key terms: shafa'a (intercession), maghfira (forgiveness), fadl (grace), rahma (mercy), hikma (wisdom). Understand how they relate to the argument.
These terms are central to the Sunni understanding of divine forgiveness.
3
Analyze the logical structure
Break down the argument: Premise – God may forgive a major sinner by His grace and mercy, and this forgiveness is within wisdom. Conclusion – Therefore, it is permissible for God to forgive through intercession.
The reasoning is that intercession is merely a means of requesting the same grace; it does not introduce a new category.
4
Reflect on the doctrinal context
Consider why this argument mattered: the Mu'tazila denied intercession for major sinners, but Sunni theology affirmed it as a manifestation of God's mercy.
This passage provides a simple yet powerful refutation of that denial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[إثبات الشفاعة] وهذه المسألة في الحقيقة هي المسألة الأولى، فإن عدنا لما جاز أن يغفر الله (٤) تعالى لصاحب الكبيرة بفضله ورحمته وكانت المغفرة تحت الحكمة جاز أن يغفر له بشفاعة الرسل والأنبياء عليهم السلام وبشفاعة الأخيار من الآباء والأبناء والأقارب والأستاذين(۹) والتلامذة وغيرهم.
[Proof of Intercession] And this issue is in reality the first issue. For if we return to (the fact that) it is permissible for Allah Almighty to forgive the committer of a major sin by His grace and mercy, and forgiveness is within wisdom, then it is permissible for Him to forgive him through the intercession of the messengers and prophets, peace be upon them, and through the intercession of the righteous from fathers, children, relatives, teachers, students, and others.
Source pages p. 413

Commentary
This passage provides a brief proof for the doctrine of intercession (shafa'a) for major sinners. The author states that this issue is essentially the same as the first issue (i.e., the permissibility of forgiveness by God's grace). The reasoning is: If it is permissible for God to forgive a major sinner directly by His grace and mercy, and such forgiveness is within divine wisdom, then it is also permissible for Him to forgive through the intercession of prophets, saints, and others. The logic is that intercession is merely a means of requesting that same grace; it does not introduce any new principle beyond the initial permissibility of forgiveness. This affirms the Sunni belief that intercession benefits believers who have committed major sins, contrary to the Mu'tazila who deny it.

Key Terms

مغفرة
فضل
رحمة
حكمة

أنبياء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Intercession is independent of God's will and operates automatically.
Correction: The passage ties intercession directly to God's prior permission and grace; it is not an independent power.

Checkpoint
What is the logical basis for the permissibility of intercession according to this passage?
A) Intercession is obligatory upon God.
B) Since forgiveness by God's grace is already permissible, forgiveness through intercession is also permissible.
C) Intercession was promised by the prophets.
D) Intercession is a separate act independent of God's will.

Key Takeaway
Intercession for major sinners is permissible because it is a means of requesting the same divine grace that can be given directly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God can forgive you directly by His mercy, why do you still seek intercession from others? Is it because you doubt His direct forgiveness, or because you honor His chosen ones? Examine your heart and commit to turning to God first before seeking any intercessor.
?
This lesson teaches that intercession is only a means—it does not add anything to God's grace. In your life, do you rely more on the hope that others will intercede for you than on your own direct relationship with God? What is one sin you will repent from today, trusting in God's direct forgiveness, while also asking the Prophet ﷺ to intercede for you in the proper manner?

Intercession: Possible Through Divine Mercy and Wisdom
Nasafi states the possibility of intercession based on God's mercy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that intercession (al-shafa'ah) is possible because God's forgiveness is based on His grace and wisdom, and that it is not limited to prophets but includes righteous believers.

Core Idea
Intercession is a mercy from God, not a limitation of His power.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi connects the discussion of intercession back to the earlier issue of the major sinner. He argues that if God's forgiveness can be granted freely by His grace and mercy, then it is even more plausible that He would grant forgiveness through the intercession of His righteous servants. This passage establishes the rational possibility of intercession and broadens its scope beyond prophets to include other believers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully

Notice that Nasafi begins by saying this issue is 'the first issue' – it is foundational.
2
Identify the logical structure
The passage has a simple argument: a premise about forgiveness by grace, a premise about wisdom, and a conclusion about intercession. Find these parts.
The reasoning is a fortiori (qiyas awlawī).
3
List the intercessors
Nasafi lists several categories of people who may intercede. Write them down and consider why he includes such a wide range.
